The Role of Trial and the Distinction Between Prevention and Waste
Maturidi distinguishes between prevention and waste, asserts necessity of trial for accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between those prevented from power and those who waste it, and why trial requires the possibility of both obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
Those who waste their power are more deserving of obligation; trial cannot exist if only the obedient are commanded.

Having refuted al-Ka'bi's narrow definition, Maturidi now addresses the principle that imposing on one who lacks energy is invalid. He distinguishes between someone prevented from having energy and someone who wastes it. The latter still deserves command because trial requires the possibility of sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note how Maturidi differentiates between 'prevented' and 'wasted'.
The phrase 'من منع عنه الطاقة' vs 'من ضيّع القوة' is key.
2
Understand the trial condition
Maturidi states that if only those who obey were commanded, that would not be a trial. Reflect on why free choice is essential.
Trial (محنة) implies both options are present.
3
Connect to Maturidi's theory of power
Maturidi explains that power arises from the servant's choice and effort. If one wastes it, power does not occur.
This aligns with the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on acquisition (كسب).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما ذكره الكعبي مما يبين وهمه في قضاياه زعم أن تكليف ما لا يطاق قبيح في العقل بالبديهة. وهذا إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهي الصحة، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول، بل كلف الله صاحب موسى بما يعلم أنه لا يستطيع مثله وكذلك تكليف ما يُجهل مثله في البديهة ، فمثله الأول. ثم يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة، وذلك وقت الفعل. فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيما ادعى . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل. وأما من ضيّع القوة فهو أحق من أن يكلف مثله؛ ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم، إذ هي تحدث تباعاً على قدر حرص العبد واختياره وميله إليها، فما لم يحدث لم تحدث بتضييعه؛ إذ اثر بدله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then we mention what al-Ka'bi mentioned, showing his error in his claims: he claimed that imposing what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) is ugly in the intellect by first principles. This is only in the intellect that knows no energy other than the apparent power, which is health. As for other than that, it is not as he says. Rather, Allah imposed on the companion of Moses what He knew he could not do similarly, and likewise imposing what is unknown like it in first principles, so it is like the first. Then it is said to him: likewise, imposing what cannot be borne at the time of the action is ugly in the intellect, and what you claimed of ugliness is only in the intellects of those who consider the occurrence of the action impossible and no power, and that is at the time of the action. So his statement upon analysis becomes that the ugly in the intellect is if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except through Allah. As for the principle that imposing on one who is prevented from energy is invalid in the intellect. As for one who wastes his power, he is more deserving to be imposed upon similarly; and if he were not imposed upon similarly, then none would be imposed upon except those who obey, and that is not the condition of trial. And there is no power except through Allah. And according to us, power in the healthy sound person occurs successively according to the measure of the servant's effort, choice, and inclination towards it. So as long as he does not bring it about, it does not occur due to his wasting it; because he chose its opposite and chose the action that repels it. And there is no power except through Allah. And upon such estimation according to us and them is the matter of understanding and knowledge. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage introduces and refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that imposing what cannot be borne is self-evidently ugly. Maturidi argues that this relies on a narrow understanding of 'energy' as mere physical health. He gives the counterexample of the companion of Moses (possibly Fir'awn), whom God knew could not believe, yet still commanded him. Thus, imposing what cannot be borne is not necessarily ugly. Maturidi then distinguishes between those prevented from power and those who waste it; the latter are still obligated because trial requires possibility of both obedience and disobedience. Finally, Maturidi states the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that power arises from the servant's choice and effort, and if he chooses the opposite, power does not occur.

Key Terms
منع
تضييع
قوة
محنة
اختيار
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imposing any command on someone unable to obey is unjust and invalid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that it is valid to impose on those who waste their power, as they are responsible for their choices; only those prevented externally are excused.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, who is more deserving of being imposed upon: someone prevented from energy or someone who wastes his strength?
A) The one prevented from energy.
B) The one who wastes his strength.
C) Both are equally undeserving.
D) Neither, because imposition is always unjust.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi distinguishes between external prevention and self-waste, affirming that trial requires commanding even those who may waste their power, because accountability rests on choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have wasted an opportunity to do good, do you ever excuse yourself by saying 'I just didn't have the motivation'? How does Maturidi's point that power arises from effort and choice challenge that excuse? Will you still say that to Allah on the Day of Judgment?
?
If trial requires commanding both obedient and disobedient, then your disobedience is part of your test. Knowing that you are being commanded precisely because you might waste power, what does that reveal about your responsibility right now for the commands you are ignoring? What will you change today?
?
You have been given power in a healthy sound body. But you have wasted it on sin. The passage says 'he is more deserving to be imposed upon similarly.' Does that make you feel burdened or accountable? Can you honestly say you have no power to obey, or have you simply chosen against it? Repent and act before the power is removed.

The Limits of Intuitive Knowledge
Maturidi critiques intuitive reason's scope regarding obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that intuitive reason only judges apparent power, not deeper incapacities.

Core Idea
Intuition alone cannot judge obligations involving hidden incapacities.

Maturidi begins by addressing an opponent who claims that reason intuitively knows obligating the impossible is ugly. He immediately qualifies this: such intuition only applies to visible, apparent power. What lies beyond—like internal states—requires deeper analysis. This lesson unpacks that first move.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Then read the translation. Note the contrast between 'the intellect that knows only apparent power' and 'other than it'.
2
Identify the two types of intellect
Maturidi distinguishes between an intellect that only recognizes outward strength (soundness) and a deeper intellect that considers hidden factors. Find this distinction in the text.
The phrase 'أما غيرها فليس كما يقول' marks the turning point.
3
Understand the example
Maturidi gives the example of the companion of Moses (likely al-Khaḍir) who was obligated with something God knew he could not do. Ask yourself: why does this example challenge the opponent’s claim?
This shows that intuitive judgment is incomplete.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Connect the example back to the core idea: intuition is not a universal arbiter of all obligations. Some obligations are known only through revelation or deeper reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل بالبديهة. وهذا إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهي الصحة، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول، بل كلف الله صاحب موسى بما يعلم أنه لا يستطيع مثله وكذلك تكليف ما يُجهل مثله في البديهة ، فمثله الأول. ثم يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة، وذلك وقت الفعل. فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيما ادعى . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل. وأما من ضيّع القوة فهو أحق من أن يكلف مثله؛ ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم، إذ هي تحدث تباعاً على قدر حرص العبد واختياره وميله إليها، فما لم يحدث لم تحدث بتضييعه؛ إذ أثر بدله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The intellect through intuition. This is only in the intellect which does not know strength except the apparent power, which is soundness. As for other than it, it is not as he says. Rather, God obligated the companion of Moses with what He knows he cannot do the like of it, and likewise the obligation of what its like is unknown in intuition, so its like is the first. Then it is said to him: Likewise, the obligation of what cannot be endured at the time of the act is rationally ugly, and what you have claimed of ugliness is only in the intellects of those who deem the existence of the act impossible and there is no power, and that is at the time of the act. So his statement upon examination is that it is rationally ugly if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except with God. As for the principle: the obligation of one from whom capacity is prevented is corrupt in the intellect. As for one who wastes his power, he is more deserving of being obligated with its like; if he were not obligated with its like, then no one would be obligated except one who obeys, and that is not the condition of trial. And there is no power except with God. And according to us, capacity is in the sound and healthy person, for it occurs successively according to the measure of the servant's striving, choice, and inclination towards it. So if he does not bring it about, it does not occur due to his wasting it, because he chose its opposite and chose the act that prevents it. And there is no power except with God. And on this estimation, according to us and according to them, is the matter of understanding and knowledge. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the nature of human capacity (qudra) and the rational status of obligating the incapable (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). Maturidi first addresses the opponent's claim that the intellect intuitively knows that obligating the impossible is ugly. He counters that this intuition only applies to the intellect that recognizes only visible, apparent power (al-quwwa al-ẓāhira, i.e., physical health). What lies beyond that – such as internal factors that prevent action – is not judged by the same intuition. He gives the example of Moses' companion (perhaps al-Khaḍir) whom God obligated with something He knew the companion could not do. Similarly, obligating something whose impossibility is unknown to the intuition is analogous to the first case. He then argues that obligating what is unbearable at the moment of action is rationally ugly, but the opponent's claimed ugliness is based on minds that assume the act is impossible and there is no power at that moment. Thus, if the opponent is correct in his claim, his own statement becomes that it is rationally ugly – but that leads to contradictions. Maturidi then lays down the foundational principle: obligating someone from whom capacity has been prevented is rationally corrupt, but obligating someone who has wasted his own power (by choosing the opposite act) is more appropriate; otherwise, only those who already obey would be obligated, which defeats the purpose of trial (miḥna). He then states the Maturidi doctrine: capacity (qudra) is in the sound, healthy person, and it occurs successively (tabā‘an) in accordance with the servant's effort, choice, and inclination. If the servant does not bring about the act, capacity does not occur because he has wasted it by choosing its opposite. The same principle applies to understanding and knowledge. This argument firmly establishes human responsibility and the conditional nature of capacity.

Key Terms
العقل بالبديهة
القوة الظاهرة
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that rational intuition is a universal judge of all obligations, including hidden incapacities.
Correction: Maturidi shows that intuition only covers apparent, physical power; deeper incapacities require other forms of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the intellect through intuition recognize?
A) All forms of power, including hidden ones
B) Only apparent power, which is soundness
C) Only the inability to perform acts
D) The ugliness of obligating the incapable in all cases

Key Takeaway
Intuitive reason is not an exhaustive judge of all obligations; it only covers visible, apparent capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that seems impossible to you, do you immediately assume it is unjust or that Allah made a mistake? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you do the next time such a thought arises?
?
Think of a time you judged someone else's religious obligation as unfair because you only saw their apparent ability. How would the example of Moses's companion change your perspective, and what concrete step can you take to avoid such judgments in the future?
?
Are there areas in your life where you rely solely on your intuition to decide what is right or wrong, ignoring that Allah may know hidden factors? What is one specific area where you will consciously submit your judgment to Allah's wisdom, even if your intuition protests?

The Principle of Obligating the Incapable
Maturidi states the rational principle for obligating the incapable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational principle: it is corrupt to obligate someone from whom capacity is prevented, but just to obligate one who wastes his own power.

Core Idea
Obligation is rational only when capacity is present; wasting capacity makes one deserving of obligation.

Having shown the limits of intuition, Maturidi now lays down a foundational principle of rational obligation. He distinguishes between two cases: someone prevented from having capacity, and someone who wastes the capacity he has. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine justice and human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the two 'as for' (أما) clauses that contrast the two types of persons.
The phrase 'وأما الأصل' signals the main principle.
2
Identify the two categories
First: the one from whom capacity is prevented (من منع عنه الطاقة). Second: the one who wastes his power (من ضيّع القوة). How does Maturidi judge obligation for each?
First is corrupt (fāsid), second is more deserving.
3
Understand the trial condition
Maturidi says if we did not obligate those who waste power, only obedient people would be obligated—contradicting the purpose of trial (miḥna). Why is this important?
Trial requires the possibility of both obedience and disobedience.
4
Reflect on divine justice
Consider how this principle protects God's justice: He does not obligate the incapable, but He does hold people accountable for squandering their ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل بالبديهة. وهذا إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهي الصحة، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول، بل كلف الله صاحب موسى بما يعلم أنه لا يستطيع مثله وكذلك تكليف ما يُجهل مثله في البديهة ، فمثله الأول. ثم يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة، وذلك وقت الفعل. فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيما ادعى . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل. وأما من ضيّع القوة فهو أحق من أن يكلف مثله؛ ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم، إذ هي تحدث تباعاً على قدر حرص العبد واختياره وميله إليها، فما لم يحدث لم تحدث بتضييعه؛ إذ أثر بدله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The intellect through intuition. This is only in the intellect which does not know strength except the apparent power, which is soundness. As for other than it, it is not as he says. Rather, God obligated the companion of Moses with what He knows he cannot do the like of it, and likewise the obligation of what its like is unknown in intuition, so its like is the first. Then it is said to him: Likewise, the obligation of what cannot be endured at the time of the act is rationally ugly, and what you have claimed of ugliness is only in the intellects of those who deem the existence of the act impossible and there is no power, and that is at the time of the act. So his statement upon examination is that it is rationally ugly if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except with God. As for the principle: the obligation of one from whom capacity is prevented is corrupt in the intellect. As for one who wastes his power, he is more deserving of being obligated with its like; if he were not obligated with its like, then no one would be obligated except one who obeys, and that is not the condition of trial. And there is no power except with God. And according to us, capacity is in the sound and healthy person, for it occurs successively according to the measure of the servant's striving, choice, and inclination towards it. So if he does not bring it about, it does not occur due to his wasting it, because he chose its opposite and chose the act that prevents it. And there is no power except with God. And on this estimation, according to us and according to them, is the matter of understanding and knowledge. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the nature of human capacity (qudra) and the rational status of obligating the incapable (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). Maturidi first addresses the opponent's claim that the intellect intuitively knows that obligating the impossible is ugly. He counters that this intuition only applies to the intellect that recognizes only visible, apparent power (al-quwwa al-ẓāhira, i.e., physical health). What lies beyond that – such as internal factors that prevent action – is not judged by the same intuition. He gives the example of Moses' companion (perhaps al-Khaḍir) whom God obligated with something He knew the companion could not do. Similarly, obligating something whose impossibility is unknown to the intuition is analogous to the first case. He then argues that obligating what is unbearable at the moment of action is rationally ugly, but the opponent's claimed ugliness is based on minds that assume the act is impossible and there is no power at that moment. Thus, if the opponent is correct in his claim, his own statement becomes that it is rationally ugly – but that leads to contradictions. Maturidi then lays down the foundational principle: obligating someone from whom capacity has been prevented is rationally corrupt, but obligating someone who has wasted his own power (by choosing the opposite act) is more appropriate; otherwise, only those who already obey would be obligated, which defeats the purpose of trial (miḥna). He then states the Maturidi doctrine: capacity (qudra) is in the sound, healthy person, and it occurs successively (tabā‘an) in accordance with the servant's effort, choice, and inclination. If the servant does not bring about the act, capacity does not occur because he has wasted it by choosing its opposite. The same principle applies to understanding and knowledge. This argument firmly establishes human responsibility and the conditional nature of capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think it is unjust for God to obligate someone who has the ability but chooses not to use it.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such obligation is actually more just because it maintains the purpose of trial and accountability.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the rational ruling for obligating someone who wastes his own power?
A) It is rationally corrupt
B) It is more deserving (أحق)
C) It is neutral
D) It is forbidden

Key Takeaway
It is rationally corrupt to obligate the incapable, but just to obligate the one who wastes his capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you known you had the capacity to do something good but wasted it through distraction or laziness? How does this lesson confront your excuses?
?
Are you living as if you are a victim of circumstances, or do you take responsibility for the capacity God has given you? What will you do today to stop wasting your power?
?
If God were to hold you accountable only for what you have done with your capacity, would you be pleased or terrified? What can you change right now?

Capacity Arises with Striving and Choice
Maturidi defines the nature of capacity as arising with the servant's choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's doctrine that human capacity (qudra) occurs successively in proportion to the servant's effort and choice.

Core Idea
Capacity is not pre-created; it arises with the servant's striving and choice.

After establishing the principle of obligation, Maturidi now explains the nature of capacity (qudra) itself. He presents the distinct Maturidi position: capacity is not a fixed entity but occurs moment by moment based on the servant's effort, choice, and inclination. This lesson unpacks that dynamic view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key phrase 'تحدث تباعاً' (occurs successively). What does this imply about the relationship between capacity and action?
2
Analyze the conditions for capacity
Maturidi lists three factors: the servant's striving (حرص), choice (اختيار), and inclination (ميل). How do these relate to the occurrence of capacity?
Capacity is not automatic; it depends on these human inputs.
3
Understand the consequence of wasting
If the servant does not bring about the act, capacity does not occur because he chose its opposite. This shows that capacity is conditional upon choice.
The phrase 'إذ أثر بدله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه' explains the mechanism.
4
Extend the principle to knowledge
Maturidi states that the same estimation applies to understanding and knowledge (أمر الفهم والعلم). How does this broaden the doctrine?
This shows the principle is general for all human capabilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل بالبديهة. وهذا إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهي الصحة، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول، بل كلف الله صاحب موسى بما يعلم أنه لا يستطيع مثله وكذلك تكليف ما يُجهل مثله في البديهة ، فمثله الأول. ثم يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة، وذلك وقت الفعل. فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيما ادعى . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل. وأما من ضيّع القوة فهو أحق من أن يكلف مثله؛ ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم، إذ هي تحدث تباعاً على قدر حرص العبد واختياره وميله إليها، فما لم يحدث لم تحدث بتضييعه؛ إذ أثر بدله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The intellect through intuition. This is only in the intellect which does not know strength except the apparent power, which is soundness. As for other than it, it is not as he says. Rather, God obligated the companion of Moses with what He knows he cannot do the like of it, and likewise the obligation of what its like is unknown in intuition, so its like is the first. Then it is said to him: Likewise, the obligation of what cannot be endured at the time of the act is rationally ugly, and what you have claimed of ugliness is only in the intellects of those who deem the existence of the act impossible and there is no power, and that is at the time of the act. So his statement upon examination is that it is rationally ugly if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except with God. As for the principle: the obligation of one from whom capacity is prevented is corrupt in the intellect. As for one who wastes his power, he is more deserving of being obligated with its like; if he were not obligated with its like, then no one would be obligated except one who obeys, and that is not the condition of trial. And there is no power except with God. And according to us, capacity is in the sound and healthy person, for it occurs successively according to the measure of the servant's striving, choice, and inclination towards it. So if he does not bring it about, it does not occur due to his wasting it, because he chose its opposite and chose the act that prevents it. And there is no power except with God. And on this estimation, according to us and according to them, is the matter of understanding and knowledge. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the nature of human capacity (qudra) and the rational status of obligating the incapable (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). Maturidi first addresses the opponent's claim that the intellect intuitively knows that obligating the impossible is ugly. He counters that this intuition only applies to the intellect that recognizes only visible, apparent power (al-quwwa al-ẓāhira, i.e., physical health). What lies beyond that – such as internal factors that prevent action – is not judged by the same intuition. He gives the example of Moses' companion (perhaps al-Khaḍir) whom God obligated with something He knew the companion could not do. Similarly, obligating something whose impossibility is unknown to the intuition is analogous to the first case. He then argues that obligating what is unbearable at the moment of action is rationally ugly, but the opponent's claimed ugliness is based on minds that assume the act is impossible and there is no power at that moment. Thus, if the opponent is correct in his claim, his own statement becomes that it is rationally ugly – but that leads to contradictions. Maturidi then lays down the foundational principle: obligating someone from whom capacity has been prevented is rationally corrupt, but obligating someone who has wasted his own power (by choosing the opposite act) is more appropriate; otherwise, only those who already obey would be obligated, which defeats the purpose of trial (miḥna). He then states the Maturidi doctrine: capacity (qudra) is in the sound, healthy person, and it occurs successively (tabā‘an) in accordance with the servant's effort, choice, and inclination. If the servant does not bring about the act, capacity does not occur because he has wasted it by choosing its opposite. The same principle applies to understanding and knowledge. This argument firmly establishes human responsibility and the conditional nature of capacity.

Key Terms
قدرة
تحدث تباعاً
حرص
اختيار
ميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that capacity is fully created by God independent of human choice, making human effort irrelevant.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that capacity occurs in accordance with the servant's striving, choice, and inclination—so human effort is integral.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, on what does the occurrence of capacity depend?
A) Only God's will, independent of human action
B) The servant's striving, choice, and inclination
C) The servant's physical health alone
D) The servant's prior knowledge

Key Takeaway
Human capacity is not pre-fixed; it arises in proportion to the servant's own effort, choice, and inclination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim you are unable to perform a good deed, have you genuinely exerted your striving and choice, or have you chosen its opposite? What specific act of disobedience have you chosen instead?
?
If capacity arises only when you strive, what does your lack of progress in certain areas of worship say about your choices? Are you willing to confront that your 'I can't' may really be 'I won't'?
?
The Maturidi teaches that capacity occurs in proportion to your own effort. How does this accountability affect your view of your own spiritual failures? Will you today take one concrete step that you have been avoiding, knowing that capacity will arise with your choice?

The Quranic Principle of Capacity and the Objector's Error
Maturidi cites the Quranic verse and explains the error in the objector's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector's claim that obligation is dropped at the time of action contradicts the Quranic principle that Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.

Core Idea
The objector distorts 'capacity' by nullifying ability at the moment of action.

Imam Maturidi begins this passage by affirming the correct interpretation of Quran 2:286 and exposing the ugliness of the objector's reasoning. The objector argues that obligation is lifted at the moment of action and ability is annulled, effectively making the act beyond one's capacity. Maturidi points out the contradiction in this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the direct reference to the Quranic verse.
The verse is Quran 2:286, a key text on taklif.
2
Understand the Quranic principle
The verse states that Allah does not burden any soul beyond its capacity. This implies that obligation is always within human ability.
3
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says that at the time of action, the obligation is dropped and ability is nullified. This is the core error.
The objector's position is called 'isqat al-taklif' (dropping of obligation).
4
Recognize Maturidi's criticism
Maturidi declares this claim 'ugly' because it makes the act beyond capacity, contradicting the verse.
The contradiction is between the verse's guarantee and the objector's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا تأويل قوله : لا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها ﴾ [البقرة، ٢٨٦/٢]، وبينا قبح المكلّف قوله في إسقاط التكليف وقت الفعل وإبطال القدرة عليه فيصير في التحصيل هو على غير الوسع . على أنه يقال : كيف لو كان في علم الله أنه لا يفعل، أو في علمه أنه يريد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه ؟ ومن قولكم: إن من أراد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه فإنه يفعله لا محالة، إلا أن يُمنع أن يفعله؛ أيُمنع أو يفعل ضده؟ فإن قال: يفعل ضده، أبطل قوله في كون الإرادة قبل الفعل وألزم نفسه الأمر معه والقدرة معه وبطل قولهم في الإرادة الموجبة. وإن قال : يُمنع، فقد ألزم من في علم الله أنه لا يفعل التكليف بقوة تُمنع عن الفعل، وهو في التحقيق تكليف العاجز الممنوع. وإن قال : ترتفع الكلفة، أبطل أن يكون أحد ـ ممن في علم الله أنه لا يطيعه - مما تضمنته المحنة ولزمه الأمر والنهي، وذلك غاية ما ينتهي إليه القول في القبح. وعلى ذلك أمر الإرادة، إن الله إذ يمنعه عن الفعل الذي في علمه أنه لا يفعله لا بد أن يمنعه عن الإرادة ، وفي ذلك منع عن الطاعة عنده والخير. ثم يقال: المروي عن الذي روى أنه سل سيفه على رسول الله فمنعه الله بقبض يده، أكان ذلك المنع أصلح له في الدين وأخير له أو الإطلاق؟ فإن قال: الإطلاق، فقد أقر بأن الله قد يفعل بعباده ما كان غيره أصلح لهم في الدين؛ وإن قال: المنع، فقد أقر أن المنع قد يكون أصلح؛ فكل عاص لم يمنع عنه لم يفعل به الأصلح. ولا قوة إلا بالله. واحتجاجه بقوله : ولو استطعنا لخرجنا معكم ﴾ [التوبة، ٤٢/٩]، قد بينا ما عليه في ذلك، وما يُظهر أن خصمه أشد لاتباع ذلك ومعرفته منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم عارض بما لا يُحقق لهم العذر بالفقر إلا بما لا يتهيأ لهم الفعل، وذلك المعنى في فقد القدرة موجود.
And we have explained the interpretation of His saying: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' [Quran 2:286], and we have explained the ugliness of the objector's statement in dropping the obligation at the time of the act and nullifying the ability for it, so it becomes in reality beyond capacity. Moreover, it is said: How would it be if it were in God's knowledge that he will not act, or in His knowledge that he intends to act at the time that follows? And from your statement: Whoever intends the act at the time that follows, then he does it inevitably, unless he is prevented from doing it. Is he prevented or does he do its opposite? If he says: He does its opposite, he nullifies his statement concerning will before the act and imposes upon himself the command with it and ability with it, and their statement about necessitating will becomes void. And if he says: He is prevented, then he has imposed upon the one in God's knowledge that he will not act the obligation with a force that is prevented from the act, and in reality it is the obligation of the incapacitated, prevented one. And if he says: The burden is removed, he nullifies that anyone of those in God's knowledge that he will not obey Him is included in the trial, and the command and prohibition apply to him, and that is the ultimate limit of the statement about ugliness. And similarly concerning will: indeed God, when He prevents him from the act that He knows he will not do, must prevent him from the will, and in that is prevention from obedience and good with Him. Then it is said: The narrated from the one who reported that he drew his sword against the Messenger of God, and God prevented him by seizing his hand, was that prevention more beneficial for him in religion and better for him, or letting go? If he says: Letting go, then he has admitted that God may do to His servants what is other than the more beneficial for them in religion; and if he says: Prevention, then he has admitted that prevention may be more beneficial; so every disobedient one who was not prevented has not had the more beneficial done to him. And there is no power except with God. And his argument with His saying: 'If we had been able, we would have gone out with you' [Quran 9:42], we have explained what we hold regarding that, and what shows that his opponent is more severe in following that and knowing it than him. And there is no power except with God. Then he objected by that which does not verify the excuse for them by poverty except by that which they cannot do the act, and that meaning is present in the absence of ability.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument concerning divine obligation (taklif) and human ability (qudra). The author (Imam Maturidi) begins by stating that he has already explained the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286) and has demonstrated the ugliness of the opponent's claim that obligation is dropped at the time of action and that ability is nullified, so that in reality the act is beyond one's capacity. He then poses a hypothetical: what if it is in God's knowledge that the person will not act, or that he intends to act at a subsequent time? He then examines the opponent's own principle: whoever intends an action at a subsequent time must perform it unless prevented. He asks: is this person prevented from the act, or does he do its opposite? If the opponent says he does the opposite, then the opponent contradicts his own claim that will precedes the act and imposes command and ability simultaneously, nullifying the idea of a necessitating will. If the opponent says he is prevented, then he is imposing an obligation on someone whom God knows will not act, but with a force that is prevented—which is truly an obligation on the incapacitated and prevented. If the opponent says the burden is lifted, then he nullifies that anyone whom God knows will not obey is subject to trial and command/prohibition, which is the extreme of ugliness in argument. The author then extends this reasoning to will (irada): if God prevents the act He knows will not be done, He must also prevent the will to it, which in turn prevents obedience and good. Next, the author introduces an anecdote about a man who drew his sword against the Prophet and was prevented by God seizing his hand. He asks whether that prevention was more beneficial (aslah) for him in religion than letting go. If the opponent says letting go, he admits God may do what is not more beneficial; if he says prevention, he admits prevention can be more beneficial. Thus, every disobedient person who is not prevented has not received the more beneficial treatment. Finally, the author mentions the opponent's proof from Quran 9:42 ('If we had been able...') and another objection concerning poverty as an excuse, concluding that the absence of ability (faqd al-qudra) is the same meaning present.

Key Terms
تكليف
وسع
إسقاط التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'capacity' refers only to physical ability, but the objector's error is about the timing of ability.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the objector's claim nullifies ability at the moment of action, making the act beyond capacity, which is a misuse of the verse.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about obligation at the time of the act?
A) It is affirmed.
B) It is dropped.
C) It is doubled.
D) It is deferred.

Key Takeaway
The objector's position is self-contradictory because it nullifies ability at the moment of action, making the act beyond the capacity that the verse guarantees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a general Quranic principle to excuse yourself from a specific command that felt too heavy? How does this lesson expose the danger of such reasoning?
?
When you face a difficult obligation from Allah, do you focus on your own momentary weakness or on the truth that Allah never burdens beyond capacity? Which response leads to action, and which leads to inaction?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's commands are always within your capacity, what would you do differently today? What one command have you been avoiding that you now realize is within your reach?

The Logical Dilemma of Divine Knowledge and Will
Maturidi presents a logical dilemma on will and divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the opponent's claims about will, prevention, and divine knowledge, and see how each possible response leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
The opponent's own logic forces him into absurdities no matter which option he chooses.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a rigorous hypothetical to expose the flaws in the objector's position. He asks: If God knows that a person will not act, or knows that he intends to act at a later time, then what? The opponent's own principle states that whoever intends an act at a subsequent time will inevitably do it unless prevented. Maturidi then examines three possible responses to the question of whether the person is prevented or not, each leading to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. This is a dense logical argument.
Pay attention to the hypothetical scenario introduced by 'كيف لو كان'.
2
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Maturidi posits that it is in God's knowledge that the person will not act, or that he intends to act at a future time. The opponent's own principle is that intention leads to inevitable action unless prevented.
3
List the three possible answers
Maturidi asks: is the person prevented, or does he do the opposite, or is the burden lifted? Identify each one in the text.
The Arabic keywords: يفعل ضده, يُمنع, ترتفع الكلفة.
4
Analyze each consequence
For each answer, note the contradiction that arises: opposite nullifies will before act; prevented implies obligation of the incapacitated; burden lifted exempts from trial.
Each consequence is meant to show the opponent's position is incoherent.
5
See the extension to will
Maturidi extends the reasoning: if God prevents the act, He must also prevent the will, which prevents obedience and good.
This shows the opponent's view on will is also problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the opponent says the person does the opposite of the intended act?
A) He affirms that the act will be done.
B) He nullifies his statement on will before the act and imposes command and ability.
C) He proves that the person is incapacitated.
D) He acknowledges the burden is lifted.

Key Takeaway
The opponent is forced into logical absurdities; the only consistent position is to affirm God's knowledge and will without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi shows that if God knows you will not obey, you are still commanded to obey. Does this confront your own tendency to blame destiny ('whatever will be, will be') when you fall short? Will you instead lean into the command as if your choice matters—because before God, it does?
?
The passage argues that prevention from sin includes prevention of the will to sin. Do you ever excuse your sins by saying 'I couldn't help it' or 'God willed it' while ignoring that He also gives you moments of free will? What would it cost you to take full ownership of your disobedience today?
?
Maturidi forces you to see the logical dead ends of denying God's will. When have you, even silently, reasoned that God's knowledge or will is unfair? Are you willing to surrender your logic to the truth that God's decree is perfect, even when your mind cannot grasp it?

The Sword Anecdote and the Concept of Aslah
Maturidi uses an anecdote to argue about aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the anecdote of the man who drew his sword against the Prophet illustrates the concept of aslah (most beneficial) and refutes the objector's position.

Core Idea
Prevention can be more beneficial; the objector's admission leads to theological inconsistency.

Imam Maturidi narrates a well-known anecdote about a man who attempted to harm the Prophet and was miraculously prevented by God seizing his hand. He then poses a dilemma to the opponent: was this prevention more beneficial (aslah) for the man in religion, or would letting him act have been better? Either answer undermines the opponent's theological assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. This is a concrete example from tradition.
The anecdote involves a man drawing his sword against the Prophet.
2
Understand the two possible answers
Maturidi asks: was the prevention more beneficial (aslah) or would letting go (itlaq) have been better?
The two answers are 'al-man' (prevention) and 'al-itlaq' (letting go).
3
Analyze the implications of each answer
If the opponent says letting go is better, he admits God may do what is not most beneficial. If he says prevention is better, he admits prevention can be beneficial, implying that those not prevented lack the best.
Both options contradict the objector's own theological commitments.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This anecdote shows that God's prevention can be a mercy, and the objector cannot consistently maintain his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا تأويل قوله : لا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها ﴾ [البقرة، ٢٨٦/٢]، وبينا قبح المكلّف قوله في إسقاط التكليف وقت الفعل وإبطال القدرة عليه فيصير في التحصيل هو على غير الوسع . على أنه يقال : كيف لو كان في علم الله أنه لا يفعل، أو في علمه أنه يريد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه ؟ ومن قولكم: إن من أراد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه فإنه يفعله لا محالة، إلا أن يُمنع أن يفعله؛ أيُمنع أو يفعل ضده؟ فإن قال: يفعل ضده، أبطل قوله في كون الإرادة قبل الفعل وألزم نفسه الأمر معه والقدرة معه وبطل قولهم في الإرادة الموجبة. وإن قال : يُمنع، فقد ألزم من في علم الله أنه لا يفعل التكليف بقوة تُمنع عن الفعل، وهو في التحقيق تكليف العاجز الممنوع. وإن قال : ترتفع الكلفة، أبطل أن يكون أحد ـ ممن في علم الله أنه لا يطيعه - مما تضمنته المحنة ولزمه الأمر والنهي، وذلك غاية ما ينتهي إليه القول في القبح. وعلى ذلك أمر الإرادة، إن الله إذ يمنعه عن الفعل الذي في علمه أنه لا يفعله لا بد أن يمنعه عن الإرادة ، وفي ذلك منع عن الطاعة عنده والخير. ثم يقال: المروي عن الذي روى أنه سل سيفه على رسول الله فمنعه الله بقبض يده، أكان ذلك المنع أصلح له في الدين وأخير له أو الإطلاق؟ فإن قال: الإطلاق، فقد أقر بأن الله قد يفعل بعباده ما كان غيره أصلح لهم في الدين؛ وإن قال: المنع، فقد أقر أن المنع قد يكون أصلح؛ فكل عاص لم يمنع عنه لم يفعل به الأصلح. ولا قوة إلا بالله. واحتجاجه بقوله : ولو استطعنا لخرجنا معكم ﴾ [التوبة، ٤٢/٩]، قد بينا ما عليه في ذلك، وما يُظهر أن خصمه أشد لاتباع ذلك ومعرفته منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم عارض بما لا يُحقق لهم العذر بالفقر إلا بما لا يتهيأ لهم الفعل، وذلك المعنى في فقد القدرة موجود.
And we have explained the interpretation of His saying: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' [Quran 2:286], and we have explained the ugliness of the objector's statement in dropping the obligation at the time of the act and nullifying the ability for it, so it becomes in reality beyond capacity. Moreover, it is said: How would it be if it were in God's knowledge that he will not act, or in His knowledge that he intends to act at the time that follows? And from your statement: Whoever intends the act at the time that follows, then he does it inevitably, unless he is prevented from doing it. Is he prevented or does he do its opposite? If he says: He does its opposite, he nullifies his statement concerning will before the act and imposes upon himself the command with it and ability with it, and their statement about necessitating will becomes void. And if he says: He is prevented, then he has imposed upon the one in God's knowledge that he will not act the obligation with a force that is prevented from the act, and in reality it is the obligation of the incapacitated, prevented one. And if he says: The burden is removed, he nullifies that anyone of those in God's knowledge that he will not obey Him is included in the trial, and the command and prohibition apply to him, and that is the ultimate limit of the statement about ugliness. And similarly concerning will: indeed God, when He prevents him from the act that He knows he will not do, must prevent him from the will, and in that is prevention from obedience and good with Him. Then it is said: The narrated from the one who reported that he drew his sword against the Messenger of God, and God prevented him by seizing his hand, was that prevention more beneficial for him in religion and better for him, or letting go? If he says: Letting go, then he has admitted that God may do to His servants what is other than the more beneficial for them in religion; and if he says: Prevention, then he has admitted that prevention may be more beneficial; so every disobedient one who was not prevented has not had the more beneficial done to him. And there is no power except with God. And his argument with His saying: 'If we had been able, we would have gone out with you' [Quran 9:42], we have explained what we hold regarding that, and what shows that his opponent is more severe in following that and knowing it than him. And there is no power except with God. Then he objected by that which does not verify the excuse for them by poverty except by that which they cannot do the act, and that meaning is present in the absence of ability.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument concerning divine obligation (taklif) and human ability (qudra). The author (Imam Maturidi) begins by stating that he has already explained the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286) and has demonstrated the ugliness of the opponent's claim that obligation is dropped at the time of action and that ability is nullified, so that in reality the act is beyond one's capacity. He then poses a hypothetical: what if it is in God's knowledge that the person will not act, or that he intends to act at a subsequent time? He then examines the opponent's own principle: whoever intends an action at a subsequent time must perform it unless prevented. He asks: is this person prevented from the act, or does he do its opposite? If the opponent says he does the opposite, then the opponent contradicts his own claim that will precedes the act and imposes command and ability simultaneously, nullifying the idea of a necessitating will. If the opponent says he is prevented, then he is imposing an obligation on someone whom God knows will not act, but with a force that is prevented—which is truly an obligation on the incapacitated and prevented. If the opponent says the burden is lifted, then he nullifies that anyone whom God knows will not obey is subject to trial and command/prohibition, which is the extreme of ugliness in argument. The author then extends this reasoning to will (irada): if God prevents the act He knows will not be done, He must also prevent the will to it, which in turn prevents obedience and good. Next, the author introduces an anecdote about a man who drew his sword against the Prophet and was prevented by God seizing his hand. He asks whether that prevention was more beneficial (aslah) for him in religion than letting go. If the opponent says letting go, he admits God may do what is not more beneficial; if he says prevention, he admits prevention can be more beneficial. Thus, every disobedient person who is not prevented has not received the more beneficial treatment. Finally, the author mentions the opponent's proof from Quran 9:42 ('If we had been able...') and another objection concerning poverty as an excuse, concluding that the absence of ability (faqd al-qudra) is the same meaning present.

Key Terms
أصلح
منع
إطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God always allows free action, but the anecdote shows that prevention can be a mercy.
Correction: Maturidi uses the anecdote to argue that the objector's own admission about aslah leads to inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the opponent says that letting go (not preventing) is more beneficial for the man, what does that imply?
A) That God always does what is most beneficial.
B) That God may do what is not the most beneficial for His servants in religion.
C) That prevention is always beneficial.
D) That the man deserved punishment.

Key Takeaway
The objector's position forces him to either admit that God may not do what is most beneficial or that those not prevented lack the most beneficial treatment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were prevented from committing a sin. Did you initially feel frustrated or grateful? How does this lesson reframe that prevention as a mercy from God, and how should it change your reaction to future hindrances?
?
When you see others committing sins while you are protected, do you ever feel self-righteous? This lesson suggests that God's prevention is an act of grace. How does this realization humble you and deepen your gratitude for His guidance?
?
If you believe God sometimes withholds what seems good for you, does your trust in His wisdom waver? The anecdote challenges the assumption that God must always make obedience easy. Will you now embrace the unseen wisdom behind His decisions, even when they contradict your desires?

Refuting the Opponent's Proof from the Quran and the Excuse of Poverty
Maturidi concludes by addressing the opponent's scriptural proof and the poverty excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi counters the opponent's use of Quran 9:42 and the argument from poverty as an excuse for inability.

Core Idea
The opponent's proof from the Quran actually highlights his own ignorance, and the poverty excuse reduces to the same as the absence of ability.

In the final part of this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses the opponent's citation of Quran 9:42 ('If we had been able, we would have gone out with you') and the argument that poverty excuses inability. Maturidi maintains that he has already explained the correct interpretation, and that the opponent is actually more deserving of blame. He also notes that the excuse of poverty is only valid if the person genuinely cannot act, which is precisely the meaning of absence of ability (faqd al-qudra).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English. Note the Quranic citation and the mention of poverty.
The verse is from Surah al-Tawbah (9:42).
2
Understand the opponent's proof
The opponent uses Quran 9:42 to argue that if someone is unable, they are excused from obligation. They also claim poverty as an excuse.
3
Maturidi's response to the Quranic proof
Maturidi says he has already explained the correct interpretation, and that the opponent's knowledge is inferior.
He implies the opponent misunderstands the verse.
4
Analyze the poverty excuse
Maturidi argues that the excuse of poverty only holds if it renders the person unable to act, which is exactly the same as the absence of ability (faqd al-qudra) already discussed.
Thus the opponent's objection adds nothing new.

Maturidi's Threefold Answer on Ability and Obligation
Maturidi presents three aspects of the answer to an objection about ability and obligation, then addresses a further objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three-part response to objections about ability and obligation, and how they distinguish between different causes of inability.

Core Idea
Lack of means and wasted ability are not the same—they have different causes and effects.

Imam Maturidi addresses a recurring objection in kalam: if ability is required for obligation, then those without ability should not be obligated. He provides three distinct aspects of an answer, each highlighting a different dimension of the relationship between ability, cause, and obligation. This passage also shows how the same three aspects can be used to counter a further objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Before reading the answer, consider the objection: a person without ability cannot be obliged. How does Maturidi reframe this?
2
Explore the three aspects
Read each of the three aspects carefully. Note how each one uses a different logical angle: the first on reaching the threshold, the second on wasting choice, the third on the existence of cause.
3
See the application to a further objection
Maturidi then applies the same three aspects to another objection about giving wealth while commanding to go out.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself: What is Maturidi's fundamental point about ability and obligation? How does this relate to divine justice?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : جوابه من أوجه ثلاثة أحدها الذي معه من المال لو لم يُبلغه لم يفرض عليه، وقوة فعل البلوغ لم تكن معه، فلم يمنع الفرض؛ فمثله أمر وجود الأمرين محال وعدمها. وأيضاً إن الأمر المعتاد أن تحدث القوى تباعاً على قدر ما يختاره العبد ويريد من الفعل، فهي تحدث لا محالة، إلا أن يُضيعها هو بصرف الاختيار إلى غير ما يفعل بها، فبالتضييع عدم هذه القدرة؛ والأخرى بالمنع، لذلك اختلفا. والثالث إجازة وجود الفعل في حال لا قدرة فيها ولا يجوز في حال لا سبب به تحدث القدرة، ثبت أن أحد الوجهين ليس بنظير للأخر. والله الموفق. ثم عارض بجواز الأمر بالخروج على أن يعطوا المال معه، وزعم أنه إن أجاز أبطل قوله، وإن لم يُجز ترك قوله. فيجاب في هذا بالأوجه الثلاثة : من التفريق بالمبلغ وغير المبلغ، وبالأمر المعتاد، وبما الفعل لوقت عدم الأسباب ولا يُنكر لوقت عدم القوة؛ ولما أحد الوجهين يعدم لا به والآخر لا. ولو كان يعلم أيضا حدوث الأملاك على التتابع بخبر الصادق لكان الجواب فيهما لا يختلف. ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما في المعارضة - إذا حققت - إحالة، وهو أن تصريف المال مع الملك له لا يحتمل.
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: His answer is from three aspects. First: the one who has wealth if it did not reach him (the threshold of obligation), it would not be obligatory upon him, and the power of reaching was not with him, so he did not prevent the obligation; so his case is that the existence of both matters is impossible and their non-existence. Second: also, the customary matter is that powers occur consecutively according to what the servant chooses and intends of the act, so they occur inevitably, unless he wastes them by diverting choice to other than that which is done by them; so by wasting, this ability is lost; and the other (ability) is prevented, therefore they differ. Third: the allowance of the occurrence of an act in a state where there is no ability, and it is not allowed in a state where there is no cause by which ability arises; it is established that one of the two aspects is not analogous to the other. And God is the granter of success. Then he objected by permitting the command to go out on condition that they give the wealth with him, and he claimed that if he (the respondent) permits it, he nullifies his statement, and if he does not permit, he abandons his statement. So it is answered in this with the three aspects: by the distinction between the one who reaches the threshold and the one who does not, by the customary matter, and by the fact that the act is for a time of absence of causes and not denied for a time of absence of power; and because one of the two aspects ceases not by it, and the other does not. And if he also knew the occurrence of possessions consecutively by the truthful report, the answer regarding them would not differ. And there is no power except with God. And with what is in the objection—if it is considered—is impossibility, and that is that disposal of wealth with ownership is not to be borne.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a structured answer by Imam Maturidi (the Shaykh) to an objection concerning ability (qudra) and obligation (taklif). He presents three aspects (wujuh) of the reply. The first aspect: a person who has wealth but does not have the means to reach the threshold of obligation (e.g., enough money for travel) is not obliged; the power of reaching is absent, so no prevention of obligation occurs—the existence of both the means and the obligation is impossible. The second aspect: the customary way is that powers (quwa) arise successively according to the servant's choice and intention. They arise inevitably unless the servant wastes them by turning his choice to something else; then by that wasting, ability is lost. In the other scenario, ability is prevented by an external factor; thus the two cases differ. The third aspect: it is allowed for an act to occur in a state where there is no ability (since ability precedes the act), but not allowed in a state where there is no cause by which ability arises. So the two cases are not analogous. Then a further objection is raised: the opponent objects by permitting the command to go out (like jihad) on condition that they give wealth along with it. The opponent claims that if the respondent permits this, it nullifies his own statement; if he does not permit, he abandons his statement. The answer is given using the same three aspects: distinguishing between one who reaches the threshold and one who does not, the customary matter, and the fact that the act is appropriate when there is a time of absence of causes, and is not denied when there is a time of absence of power. The two aspects (the two different states) are not identical: one ceases due to one cause, the other not. Furthermore, if the opponent also knew that possessions are given consecutively by the truthful report (revelation), the answer would not differ. Finally, the author notes that the objection, when examined, involves an impossibility: that disposal of wealth with ownership (over it) is not to be borne (i.e., it is not analogous).

Key Terms
وجه
قوة
قدرة
اختيار
تضييع
سبب
إجازة
منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that inability due to lack of means is the same as inability due to misuse of ability.
Correction: The author distinguishes them: in the first, the power was never present; in the second, the power was present but wasted by the servant's choice.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of the answer, why is a person who has wealth but does not reach the threshold of obligation not obliged?
A) Because he lacks the will to act.
B) Because the power of reaching was not with him, so the obligation is not prevented but simply not applicable.
C) Because God knows he will not act.
D) Because he is excused by poverty.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that different causes of inability lead to different rulings, and the same three aspects serve to refute multiple objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever wasted an ability God gave you by choosing to focus on lesser things, only to find yourself incapable when a real obligation came? What will you change today to stop that waste?
?
When you are unable to fulfill a duty, do you honestly examine whether it was due to your own choices or external factors? How might this lesson change the way you account for yourself?
?
The passage teaches that powers arise according to what we choose and intend. If you were to evaluate your daily intentions, what would they reveal about the abilities you are gaining or losing? Will you commit to directing your choices toward what truly matters?

First Aspect: Wealth Threshold and Obligation
First aspect: distinguishing sufficient from insufficient wealth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi's first aspect distinguishes between sufficient and insufficient wealth in determining obligation and power.

Core Idea
Obligation requires wealth to reach the threshold; power arises with the act.

The first aspect of Maturidi's three-fold answer addresses a common confusion: if the wealth a person possesses does not meet the required amount (mublagh), no obligation is imposed, and the power needed to perform the obligation is absent. This establishes a clear criterion for when obligation applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the first aspect passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation, focusing on the condition of wealth not reaching the required amount.
This is the first of three distinct answers.
2
Identify the key concepts
Note the terms 'mublagh' (sufficient amount) and 'qudra' (power). Understand how they relate to obligation (fard).
3
Reflect on the analogy
The author mentions that the existence of both wealth and obligation is impossible if wealth is insufficient. Consider why this logical point is important.
This is a rational judgment about impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : جوابه من أوجه ثلاثة أحدها أن الذي معه من المال لو لم يُبلغه لم يفرض عليه ، وقوة فعل البلوغ لم تكن معه ، فلم يمنع الفرض؛ فمثله أمر وجود الأمرين محال وعدمها. وأيضاً إن الأمر المعتاد أن تحدث القوى تباعاً على قدر ما يختاره العبد ويريد من الفعل، فهي تحدث لا محالة، إلا أن يُضيعها هو بصرف الاختيار إلى غير ما يفعل بها، فبالتضييع عدم هذه القدرة؛ والأخرى بالمنع، لذلك اختلفا. والثالث إجازة وجود الفعل في حال لا قدرة فيها ولا يجوز في حال لا سبب به تحدث القدرة ، ثبت أن أحد الوجهين ليس بنظير للأخر . والله الموفق. ثم عارض بجواز الأمر بالخروج على أن يعطوا المال معه، وزعم أنه إن أجاز أبطل قوله، وإن لم يُجز ترك قوله. فيجاب في هذا بالأوجه الثلاثة : من التفريق بالمبلغ وغير المبلغ، وبالأمر المعتاد، وبما الفعل لوقت عدم الأسباب ولا يُنكر لوقت عدم القوة؛ ولما أحد الوجهين يعدم لا به والآخر لا. ولو كان يعلم أيضا حدوث الأملاك على التتابع بخبر الصادق لكان الجواب فيهما لا يختلف. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: His answer has three aspects: First, that the wealth he possesses, if it does not reach [the requisite amount], is not obligatory upon him, and the power of the act of reaching was not with him, so it does not prevent the obligation; similarly, the matter of existence of both is impossible and their non-existence. Also, the customary matter is that powers occur successively according to what the servant chooses and intends of the action; they occur inevitably, unless he wastes them by directing his choice to other than that which he does with them. So by wasting, this capacity is absent; and the other is by prevention, therefore they differ. Third, allowing the existence of the act in a state where there is no capacity, and it is not allowed in a state where there is no cause by which capacity arises. So it is established that one of the two aspects is not analogous to the other. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Then he objected by allowing the command to go out provided they give the wealth with him, and he claimed that if he allows it, his statement is invalidated, and if he does not allow, his statement is abandoned. So it is answered in this by the three aspects: by distinguishing between the one who has reached [the amount] and the one who has not, by the customary matter, and by the act at the time of the absence of causes is not denied at the time of absence of power; and because one of the two aspects is absent not by it, and the other is not. And if he also knew the occurrence of possessions successively by the report of the truthful, the answer in both would not differ. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents a three-fold answer to an objection regarding the relationship between wealth, obligation, and capacity (qudra). The first aspect distinguishes between having sufficient wealth to meet the obligation and not; if the wealth is insufficient, no capacity is required, and the obligation does not arise. The second aspect discusses the customary order: powers arise progressively according to the servant's choice, and if the servant wastes them by choosing otherwise, capacity is lost. The third aspect allows that an act may occur in a state without capacity when capacity arises later, but not without a cause. Then an objection is raised: if one allows the command to expend wealth alongside participation, then either the objector's position is invalidated or abandoned. The response reasserts the three aspects, emphasizing that the two cases are not analogous. The passage guards against conflating different types of power and obligation.

Key Terms
قدرة
فرض
بلغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that obligation always requires power, regardless of wealth.
Correction: The first aspect shows that if wealth does not reach the threshold, no obligation exists, so power is not relevant.

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect, what is the condition for a financial obligation to be imposed?
A) The person must have some wealth.
B) The wealth must reach the specified amount (mublagh).
C) The person must have the power to pay.
D) The obligation is always present regardless.

Key Takeaway
The first aspect teaches that obligation is conditional on wealth reaching the required amount; if not, no obligation and no need for power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a time when you avoided a religious duty (like zakat or Hajj) because you felt you did not have the strength or willpower, even though your wealth had actually reached the required threshold? What does this passage reveal about the excuse you were making?
?
This passage teaches that obligation is tied to objective conditions (wealth threshold), not to your subjective feelings of capability. Where in your life have you been using your feeling of inadequacy as a shield against a duty that you do have the means to fulfill? What would it take for you to stop hiding behind that feeling and act?
?
As you reflect on your financial obligations in Islam, have you ever intentionally kept your wealth below the threshold to avoid the obligation? How does this passage challenge your intention? What change in your heart do you need to make to align with the truth that obligation is a mercy, not a burden?

Second Aspect: Customary Occurrence of Powers
Second aspect: role of choice in the occurrence of powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second aspect, which explains how powers arise according to the servant's choice and how waste leads to absence of capacity.

Core Idea
Powers arise successively based on choice; wasting choice leads to loss of capacity.

The second aspect of Maturidi's answer shifts from wealth to the nature of power itself. It explains that powers normally occur in succession according to what the servant intends to do. If the servant redirects his choice elsewhere, he wastes the power, causing it to be absent.

Guided Path
1
Read the second aspect passage
Read the Arabic text and translation about the customary occurrence of powers.
This aspect discusses a different type of power, related to choice.
2
Understand the mechanism
Note that powers happen successively (تحدث تباعاً) based on the servant's choice and will. They occur inevitably unless the servant wastes them by choosing differently.
This implies that capacity is not static but dynamic.
3
Contrast with the first aspect
Consider how this aspect differs: here, the focus is on the use of power, not the presence of wealth.
The two aspects are distinct answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third Aspect: Act Without Capacity vs. Without Cause
Third aspect: distinguishing absence of power from absence of cause for power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third aspect, which distinguishes between an act occurring in the absence of capacity and an act occurring without a cause for that capacity.

Core Idea
An act can occur without capacity but not without a cause for capacity.

The third aspect introduces a nuanced distinction: it is permissible for an act to exist in a state where there is no power (qudra) at the moment, but if there is no cause (sabab) that would generate that power, then the act is not permissible. This shows that the two cases are not analogous.

Guided Path
1
Read the third aspect passage
Read the Arabic and translation regarding the allowance of an act without power but not without a cause.
This is the most subtle aspect.
2
Understand the distinction
Note the difference between 'حال لا قدرة فيها' (state of no power) and 'حال لا سبب به تحدث القدرة' (state of no cause by which power occurs). The first is allowed, the second is not.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The passage concludes that one aspect is not analogous to the other, emphasizing the difference.
This is a key logical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third aspect teaches that an act can occur without immediate capacity if there is a cause that will produce that capacity.

Objection and Application of the Three Aspects
Objection and response using the three-fold answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a counter-objection is raised and then answered by applying the same three-fold distinction.

Core Idea
The three aspects consistently distinguish between different conditions of wealth, power, and cause.

After presenting the three aspects, Maturidi considers an objection: if the command to go out (for jihad) is issued on condition that they give the wealth, then either allowing the command invalidates the objector's position or not allowing it abandons the original statement. The response reapplies the three aspects to show this objection does not undermine the initial answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the objection and the answer using the three aspects.
This passage ties together the earlier aspects.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims a contradiction in the command to go out given wealth. Consider what the objection is trying to prove.
3
See how the three aspects are reapplied
The response uses the same three distinctions: distinguishing mublagh/non-mublagh, the customary matter, and the act in absence of causes.
This shows the consistency of the author's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : جوابه من أوجه ثلاثة أحدها أن الذي معه من المال لو لم يُبلغه لم يفرض عليه ، وقوة فعل البلوغ لم تكن معه ، فلم يمنع الفرض؛ فمثله أمر وجود الأمرين محال وعدمها. وأيضاً إن الأمر المعتاد أن تحدث القوى تباعاً على قدر ما يختاره العبد ويريد من الفعل، فهي تحدث لا محالة، إلا أن يُضيعها هو بصرف الاختيار إلى غير ما يفعل بها، فبالتضييع عدم هذه القدرة؛ والأخرى بالمنع، لذلك اختلفا. والثالث إجازة وجود الفعل في حال لا قدرة فيها ولا يجوز في حال لا سبب به تحدث القدرة ، ثبت أن أحد الوجهين ليس بنظير للأخر . والله الموفق. ثم عارض بجواز الأمر بالخروج على أن يعطوا المال معه، وزعم أنه إن أجاز أبطل قوله، وإن لم يُجز ترك قوله. فيجاب في هذا بالأوجه الثلاثة : من التفريق بالمبلغ وغير المبلغ، وبالأمر المعتاد، وبما الفعل لوقت عدم الأسباب ولا يُنكر لوقت عدم القوة؛ ولما أحد الوجهين يعدم لا به والآخر لا. ولو كان يعلم أيضا حدوث الأملاك على التتابع بخبر الصادق لكان الجواب فيهما لا يختلف. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

Is there a teaching or command from Allah that you have personally struggled with or rejected because it seemed contradictory to your circumstances? Would you be willing to re-examine it using the same rigorous distinctions as this passage, rather than walking away?
