Maturidi's Two Errors in the Sin Objection
Maturidi corrects two errors in an opponent's question about sin and divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical errors in the objection that if a sinner acts by God's power, the sin must be from God, and how Maturidi refutes it.

Core Idea
God grants power for obedience; sin is the servant's misuse, not from God's power.

This passage addresses a common objection about divine responsibility for sin. The opponent argues that if God creates the power to act, He must be responsible for sinful acts performed with that power. Maturidi points out two fundamental errors in this reasoning, showing that the question itself is based on false premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection, then Maturidi's identification of two errors, followed by a brief answer.
The passage is concise but packed with logical nuance.
2
Identify the first error
Maturidi says the opponent's own adversaries (خصومه) do not claim that sin is from God. This means the objection is misdirected—it attacks a position that is not held by the intended target.
3
Identify the second error
The premise 'the servant acts by God's power' is incorrect. Rather, the servant acts by a power he requested from God. This separates the power given (for obedience) from the act itself, which involves the servant's choice.
The second error shows a misunderstanding of the relationship between divine power and human action.
4
Reflect on the answer and conclusion
Maturidi refers to an earlier answer: God gave power for obedience, not for sin. The sin arises from the servant's misuse, not from the power. Consider how this resolves the question.
Understanding this distinction is key to the Maturidi position on divine power and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال: فإن قلت: العاصي إذ يفعل بقدرة الله لم لا قلت: إن المعصية من الله؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله: } وقد أخطأ من وجهين أحدهما أن خصومه لا يقولون في المعصية «إنها من الله». والثاني لا يقال: فعل العبد بقدرة الله، ولكن بقدرة طلبها من الله. ثم أجاب في ذلك بمثل جوابه في الأول: إنه أعطى ليطيع وأيم هذا. وقد بينا الوجه في الأول وخطأ [ه] في هذا السؤال.
Then he said: If you say: The sinner acts by the power of Allah, why do you not say that the sin is from Allah? The Sheikh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: He made an error in two ways: one is that his opponents do not say about sin that it is from Allah. The second is that it is not said that the servant's act is by the power of Allah, but by a power he requested from Allah. Then he answered that with an answer similar to his first: He (Allah) gave him (the power) to obey—and the like of this. And we have explained the point in the first and his error in this question.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
The opponent poses a question: if a sinner acts by God's power, why not say the sin is from God? Maturidi points out two errors. First, his own opponents (e.g., the Mu'tazila) do not say sin is from God, so the question misfires. Second, the premise is wrong: it is not said that the servant acts by God's power directly, but by a power that he requests from God—implying a distinction between the power and its application. Maturidi then refers to an earlier answer: God granted power for obedience, not for sin. The sin arises from the servant's misuse, not from the power itself.

Key Terms
معصية
قدرة الله
طلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates the power to act, He must also be responsible for the sin committed with that power.
Correction: Maturidi separates the power (granted for obedience) from the servant's misuse thereof; the sin is not from God.

Checkpoint
What is the first error Maturidi identifies in the opponent's question?
A) The opponent thinks sin is from God.
B) The opponent's own adversaries do not claim sin is from God.
C) The opponent uses a wrong analogy.
D) The opponent denies divine power.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the objection misrepresents both the opponents' position and the nature of divine power, which is granted for obedience, not for sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection on Turning Power from Good to Evil
Maturidi responds to the objection that power created for good can be turned to evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that power is created specifically for good actions and why the objection that the servant can turn it to evil is invalid.

Core Idea
Power is created for good; its misuse does not prove it was created for evil.

Maturidi presents a sophisticated defense of his position that power (qudra) is created for good actions. He anticipates an objection: if power is for good, how can the servant use it for evil? His response clarifies the nature of power as an accident that cannot be turned or remain unused, and uses common human experience to support his view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: objection followed by response.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
The opponent compares power to a sword or dirham that can be used for different purposes. Understand why this analogy seems plausible at first.
3
Analyze Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi argues that power does not admit both actions nor their omission, unlike a sword. He also points out that people ask specifically for power to do good, indicating its orientation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument defends the idea that power is created for good alone, and how the servant's misuse is a separate matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بما إذ كانت القدرة مخلوقة للخير كيف قدر العبد على قلبها؟ فزعم أن ذا ليس كالذي يُسخن ويُبرد، لكنه كالسيف والدرهم. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: } فيقال له: القدرة إذ لا تحتمل الفعلين ولا تركهما، وما عارضت به محتمل ثبت أن القدرة مخلوقة لأحدهما لا لهما. ثم لا يحتمل المخلوق بجهة واحدة قلبها عنها من نحو الذي ذكرت مما يسخن به ويبرد، لِمَ لا دل أنها خلقت لأحدهما، وهو ما كان [إلا] بها. ويبين لك العرف الظاهر في الخلق بسؤال القوة على الخير، ولو كانت لا تحتمل الشر لكان لا معنى لتخصيص ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself: If the power is created for good, how is the servant able to turn it? He claimed that this is not like that which heats and cools, but like the sword and the dirham. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: So it is said to him: Since power does not admit both actions nor their omission, and what you objected with is possible, it is established that power is created for one of them, not both. Then the created thing with one aspect does not admit being turned from it like what you mentioned of that which heats and cools—why does it not indicate that it was created for one of them, namely that which is [only] through it? And the apparent custom in creation makes clear to you by asking for power over good, and if it did not admit evil, there would be no meaning to specifying that. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
The opponent raises a new objection: if power is created specifically for good actions (as Maturidi holds), how can the servant turn it to evil? He likens it to a sword or dirham that can be used for different purposes, not to a substance that naturally heats or cools. Maturidi (Abu Mansur) responds that power by definition does not admit both actions nor complete absence of action—it must be used for one. The sword analogy fails because a sword can remain unused or be used in multiple ways, but power (qudra) is an accident that cannot persist without effect. Furthermore, if power were indifferent to good and evil, there would be no point in asking God for power to do good (since it would already be available for both). The fact that people specifically ask for power to do good shows that power is oriented toward good, but its misuse by the servant does not prove it was created for evil.

Key Terms
قدرة مخلوقة للخير
قلب
عرف ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If power is created for good, the servant cannot use it for evil.
Correction: Maturidi argues that misuse does not change the power's nature; power is still created for good, but the servant can misuse it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the sword analogy fail to explain power?
A) A sword can be used for both good and evil.
B) A sword is a physical object, not an accident.
C) Power cannot be left unused like a sword.
D) Power is created for both good and evil.

Key Takeaway
Power is created specifically for good actions; the servant's ability to use it for evil does not indicate it was created for evil, but rather shows misuse of what is essentially good.

The Capacity is Created for One Action Only: Initial Argument and Objection
Maturidi argues that capacity is for one opposite only and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that capacity is created for only one opposite and his refutation of the objection that sin would come from God if the sinner uses God's power.

Core Idea
Capacity is for one action; misusing it does not make God responsible for sin.

Maturidi presents a foundational argument about human capacity (qudra) and its relationship to actions. He asserts that capacity is created for only one of two opposites (like obedience or disobedience), and then addresses a critical objection: if a sinner uses God-given power, does that make sin from God? This passage clarifies the nuanced relationship between divine creation and human responsibility, showing why sin cannot be attributed to Allah despite His granting of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once, without overanalyzing. Notice how Maturidi moves from the initial argument to the objection and then his response.
Pay attention to the phrase 'قوة إلا بالله' and its context.
2
Identify the main argument about capacity
Maturidi states that power (qudra) can only accommodate one of two opposites. Find the sentence that expresses this and understand why he says the idea of 'repelling' (daf') is invalid.
3
Examine the objection and its two errors
The opponent asks why sin is not from God if the sinner uses God's power. Maturidi identifies two errors in this objection. List them in your own words.
The first error is about what the opponents actually claim; the second is about the nature of the power used.
4
Reflect on the answer
Maturidi answers that God gave capacity for obedience. Consider how this assigns responsibility to the servant for misuse.
5
Connect to broader theology
Think about how this argument preserves divine justice while affirming human accountability. How does it avoid attributing evil to Allah?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوة إلا بالله. وما عارض فاسد، لا حتماله أن لا يستعمل في الوجهين، فلم يكن الدفع لوجه ذلك. والقوة لا تحتمل إلا أحدهما، ولا يجوز أن يخلو عن وقوع أحدهما بها، وقد علم بذلك، فلا يحتمل القول بالدفع لغير ذلك. من ثم قال: فإن قلت: العاصي إذ يفعل بقدرة الله لم لا قلت: إن المعصية من الله؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وقد أخطأ من وجهين: أحدهما أن خصومه لا يقولون في المعصية «إنها من الله»، والثاني لا يقال: فعل العبد بقدرة الله، ولكن بقدرة طلبها من الله. ثم أجاب في ذلك بمثل جوابه في الأول: إنه أعطى ليطيع وأيم هذا. وقد بينا الوجه في الأول وخطأه في هذا السؤال.
Power except from Allah. And what is opposed is corrupt, because it is impossible that it is not used in both ways, so there was no repelling for that aspect. And power does not accept except one of them, and it is not allowed that it be free from the occurrence of one of them by it, and that is known, so the statement of repelling for other than that is not possible. Then he said: For if you say: The sinner when he acts by the power of Allah, why do you not say that disobedience is from Allah? The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: He has erred in two ways: one of them is that his opponents do not say about disobedience 'it is from Allah', and the second is that it is not said: the servant's act is by the power of Allah, but by a power requested from Allah. Then he answered that with an answer similar to his first: He gave it to obey, and what is this? And we have explained the aspect in the first and his error in this question.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that the capacity (qudra) is created for only one of two opposites (good or evil), and then addresses an objection. The author begins by stating that the capacity cannot be used for both actions and must result in one; therefore the idea of repelling (al-daf') for other than that is invalid. Then he introduces an objection from an opponent: if the sinner acts by God's power, does that make sin from God? The Shaykh (Maturidi) clarifies that the opponent is wrong in two ways: firstly, the opponents themselves do not attribute sin directly to God; secondly, the servant does not act by God's power directly, but by a power he requested from God. The answer is that God gave the capacity for obedience; the servant misuses it. The author concludes that they have already explained the first aspect and the error in this question.

Misconception: If a sinner commits sin using the power that Allah created, then sin must be from Allah.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the power used by the sinner is not directly God's power but a power that the servant requested from Allah. Allah gave it for obedience; the servant misuses it, so sin is not attributable to Allah.

Checkpoint

B) Because the servant acts by a power he requested, not directly by God's power

Key Takeaway
Capacity is created for one specific action; sin arises from the servant's misuse of granted power, not from Allah's direct involvement.

?
The objection raised suggests that if God grants power, then sin must be from Him. Do you ever hold a grudge against Allah in your heart when you fall into sin, feeling like He made it happen? How does this passage correct that attitude, and what will you do to change it?

Refuting the Sword and Dirham Analogy: Capacity is for One Action Only
Maturidi refutes the sword/dirham analogy and affirms capacity is for one action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the analogy that human capacity can be turned from good to evil like a sword, and how he argues that capacity is created for a single specific action.

Core Idea
Capacity is an accident that cannot be turned; it exists only for one action.

Maturidi anticipates a powerful objection: if capacity is created by Allah for good, how can the servant turn it to evil? The opponent compares capacity to a sword that can be used for both good and evil. Maturidi responds by clarifying that capacity is not a persistent object but an accident that only exists for the action it enables. This lesson unpacks that refutation and its implications for understanding human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read how the opponent argues that capacity can be turned like a sword from good to evil. Notice the analogy: sword/dirham can be used for either purpose.
The opponent's position: capacity is similar to tools that have multiple uses.
2
Analyze Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi argues that capacity does not admit both actions or their omission; it is created for one specific purpose. Compare this to the sword which remains after the action, while capacity is an accident that does not persist.
Key point: capacity (qudra) is an accident (arad) that exists only for the action it enables.
3
Examine the customary evidence
Maturidi points out that when we ask for power for good, we do not mean power that could also be used for evil. The very specification shows that capacity is tied to one action.
This reinforces that capacity is created for a single purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بما إذ كانت القدرة مخلوقة للخير كيف قدر العبد على قلبها؟ فزعم أن ذا ليس كالذي يُسخن ويُبرد، لكنه كالسيف والدرهم. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: فيقال له: القدرة إذ لا تحتمل الفعلين ولا تركهما، وما عارضت به محتمل ثبت أن القدرة مخلوقة لأحدهما لا لهما. ثم لا يحتمل المخلوق بجهة واحدة قلبها عنها من نحو الذي ذكرت مما يسخن به ويبرد، لِمَ لا دل أنها خلقت لأحدهما، وهو ما كان [إلا] بها. ويبين لك العرف الظاهر في الخلق بسؤال القوة على الخير، ولو كانت لا تحتمل الشر لكان لا معنى لتخصيص ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself: since capacity is created for good, how can the servant turn it? So he claimed that this is not like the one who heats and cools, but like the sword and dirham. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: So it is said to him: Capacity, since it does not admit both actions nor their omission, and what you objected with is possible, it is established that capacity is created for one of them, not for both. Then the created thing from one direction cannot be turned away from it like what you mentioned of heating and cooling, why does it not indicate that it was created for one of them, and it is only that which [was] by it? And the apparent custom in creation makes clear to you by asking about power for good, and if it did not admit evil, would there be no meaning to specifying that. And no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage addresses a further objection: if capacity is created specifically for good, how can the servant turn it to evil? The opponent argues that this is unlike a tool that heats and cools (intrinsic opposites), but is like a sword or coin that can be used for both good and evil. Abu Mansur (Imam Maturidi) refutes this by reiterating that capacity does not admit both actions nor their omission; it is created for one specific purpose. Unlike a sword which remains after use, capacity is an accident that exists only for the action. The created thing with a single function cannot be turned to its opposite. Customary usage also shows that when we ask for power for good, it implies it cannot be used for evil; otherwise specification would be meaningless.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The capacity (qudra) that enables good actions can be turned by the servant to perform evil actions.
Correction: Capacity is an accident created for a single action; it cannot be turned to its opposite because it does not persist after the action.

Checkpoint

A) Swords can only be used for evil
B) Capacity is an accident that does not remain after the action, unlike a sword
C) A sword can be used for both good and evil
D) The opponent's objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
Capacity is an accident created for one specific action; it cannot be turned to its opposite, refuting the analogy of the sword and dirham.

The Appointed Term: Refuting the Mu'tazila View
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that a servant can prevent the appointed term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila view on the appointed term leads to irrational consequences and how Maturidi refutes it with the Muslim answer based on divine knowledge and conditional decrees.

Core Idea
The appointed term is determined in Allah's knowledge, not absolutely fixed, and can vary based on actions.

This passage addresses a critical theological dispute with the Mu'tazila regarding the appointed term (ajal). The Mu'tazila claimed that a person could prevent the term set by Allah, which implies that Allah's promises can be thwarted. Maturidi presents their view and demolishes it with a logical question and a positive Muslim alternative grounded in Quran and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila say Allah sets a specific lifespan for a man, but then affirm that a servant has the power to prevent that man from reaching it. This implies that the servant can override Allah's decree.
This view seems to grant humans power over divine decrees.
2
Recognize the logical problem
If a servant can prevent the appointed term, then Allah's promise to preserve life until that term is broken, and Allah is coerced. Maturidi asks: is this not irrational?
The problem involves 'khulf al-wa'd' (breaking the promise) and qahr (coercion).
3
Learn the Muslim answer
The Muslim answer is that what occurs is in Allah's knowledge. Allah could have set a different term initially; whatever happens is according to His knowledge. The term is not absolutely fixed but is recorded in the Preserved Tablet with conditions.
This preserves Allah's power and knowledge.
4
See the evidence from Quran and hadith
The verse (Fatir 11) and hadith about family ties increasing lifespan show that the term is conditional. It is recorded in the Tablet: if he maintains ties, his term is such; if not, then such.
This supports the idea of conditional decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل عن خصمه سؤالاً بَتًره، وإنما هو - والله أعلم - أن المعتزلة تزعم أن الله تعالى يجعل لعمر الرجل مدة بها ينقضى، وإبقاؤه إلى تلك المدة فعله، وهو يريد أن يفعل ذلك، وقد كان قدر له في تلك المدة أرزاقا. ثم أثبتت لعبد من عبيد الله قوة يمنع بها ذلك الرجل عن استيفاء مدته التي جعلها الله له، ويمنع رب العالمين عن إنجاز ما وعده، ويحول بينه وبين فعله من إبقاء حياته في جسده وهو يريد ذلك؛ فيكون من فعله بما يقتل به عدوه منعا منه ربه، فيكون في ذلك خلف الوعد، وقهر ومنع عن فعله، وكل ذلك يكون بما أقدر هو عليه. وذلك لعجز وخلف سنة في المعقول أو لا؟

ثم أجاب كجواب المسلمين أن المسألة فيما يقال في كل أمر: لو لم يكن ذلك كيف كان في علم الله أن يكون؟ وذلك القول عند المسلمين على تحقيق أن الكائن في علمه ذلك، فإن كان في وقدرته أن له أن يجعل في الابتداء غير تلك المدة، ولو جعل ليكون ذلك في علمه، لا هذا الذي كان. ثم رجع إلى حقيقة قوله وقال: لو كان الظالم إنما قتل الحضور أجله لم يكن ملوماً، وقد يحمد أيضاً في ذبح شاة لآخر، إذ لولا ذبحه لكانت تموت. ثم عورض بأنك تشهد أنه لم يحضره أجله لو لم يقتله. فقال: معاذ الله، بل لعله يقتله غيره أو ينقضى أجله. ثم احتج بقوله عز وجل: {ولا يعمر من معمر ولا ينقص من عمره إلا في كتاب} [فاطر: 11]، الآية، وبقول رسول الله عليه السلام: «صلة الرحم تزيد في العمر»، فأخبر أن له مقدارا معلوماً يزيد فيه بالصلة، فيكون في اللوح: إن وصل فأجله كذا وإن لم يصل فكذا. ثم رجع إلى سفهه وعارض بالموهوم المطلق، فإن قيل: في ذلك دفع المنع لا غير، وفي القدرة الفعل؟ فقال: وفي القدرة دفع العجز لا غير. وقال: لو أوجبت القدرة لأدخلت فيه ولحملت عليه، ويكون إذ ذاك الفعل لغيري.
Then he posed to his opponent a question that cut him off, and it is - and Allah knows best - that the Mu'tazila claim that Allah makes for a man's life a period by which it ends, and His preserving him to that period is His act, and He wants to do that, and He has ordained provisions for him in that period. Then they affirm for a servant of Allah a power by which he prevents that man from completing his period which Allah has set for him, and prevents the Lord of the worlds from fulfilling His promise, and interposes between Him and His act of preserving his life in his body while He wants that; thus his action by which he kills his enemy becomes a prevention from his Lord, and in that there is breaking of promise, and coercion and prevention of His act, and all of that occurs by what He has empowered him upon. And that is incapacity and deviation from usual in the rational or not?

Then he answered with the answer of the Muslims that the matter is what is said in every thing: 'If that were not, how would it be in Allah's knowledge that it would be?' And that statement is with the Muslims upon verification that what occurs is in His knowledge accordingly, so if it is in His power that He could have made initially other than that period, and had He made it, that would be in His knowledge, not this which occurred. Then he returned to the reality of his statement and said: If the oppressor killed the one present at his appointed time, he would not be blameworthy, and he would even be praised for slaughtering a sheep for another, for if he had not slaughtered it, it would have died. Then it was objected that you testify that he did not bring his appointed time if he had not killed him. So he said: God forbid, rather perhaps someone else would kill him or his term would end. Then he argued by the saying of the Exalted: {And no person is given long life, nor is it shortened, but in a record} [Fatir: 11], and by the saying of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: 'Maintaining family ties increases life,' so he informed that it has a known amount that increases by maintaining them, so it is in the Tablet: if he maintains ties, his term is such and such, and if he does not, then such and such. Then he returned to his foolishness and objected by the absolute imagined: if it is said: in that there is repelling of prevention only, and in capacity is action? So he said: and in capacity is repelling of incapacity only. And he said: if capacity necessitated, I would enter into it and be burdened with it, and then the act would be from other than me.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents the 'Issue of the Appointed Term' (Mas'alat al-Ajal). It begins by describing the Mu'tazila position: they claim God sets a specific lifespan for a man, yet they also affirm that a servant has a power to prevent that man from reaching it, thus preventing God from fulfilling His promise. This implies breaking the promise, coercion, and prevention of God's act. Maturidi asks whether this is not irrational. Then the Muslim answer is given: the matter is understood by the principle that whatever occurs is in God's eternal knowledge. God could have initially set a different term, and if He had, that would be in His knowledge. The discussion turns to a hypothetical: if an oppressor kills someone at his appointed time, he is not blameworthy, like a person who slaughters a sheep that would have died anyway. An objection is raised that the killing caused death before the term, but the opponent says perhaps someone else would kill him or his term would end anyway. The opponent then cites Quran and hadith that lifespan can increase through good deeds, showing it is recorded in the Tablet with conditions. Finally, the opponent returns to sophistry, arguing that capacity only repels prevention, not action, and if capacity necessitated action, the agent would be compelled.

Key Terms
أجل
قدرة
خلف الوعد
صلة الرحم
اللوح المحفوظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The appointed term is absolutely fixed and unchangeable regardless of actions.
Correction: According to this passage, the term is recorded in the Preserved Tablet with conditions such as maintaining family ties; it can be extended by righteous deeds, all within Allah's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the Muslim answer in this passage, how is the appointed term determined?
A) It is fixed and unchangeable
B) It is determined in God's knowledge and may vary based on actions like maintaining family ties
C) It is solely up to human choice
D) It is randomly assigned

Key Takeaway
The appointed term is determined in Allah's knowledge and can be extended through righteous actions, refuting the Mu'tazila view that humans can override divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your lifespan is entirely in Allah's hands, or do you sometimes act as if you can secure your own safety independently of His decree? What does your reliance on plans and precautions reveal about your trust in His appointed term?
?
When you hear that maintaining family ties increases lifespan, do you dismiss it as a mere metaphor, or do you treat it as a real cause that Allah has linked to a longer life? What would change in your behavior if you genuinely believed that a specific action could extend your days?
?
If someone wrongs you, do you consider that their action is within Allah's decree and knowledge, or do you feel that they 'prevented' what Allah wanted for you? How can you reconcile your anger with the truth that nothing occurs except by His permission?

The Mu'tazila Claim: Human Power to Thwart Divine Decree
Al-Maturidi reports Al-Ka'bi's Mu'tazila argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that a person has power to prevent the fulfillment of the lifespan Allah has decreed, and the logical inconsistencies this creates.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila grant humans power to override Allah's decree, leading to absurdity.

Al-Maturidi begins by presenting the Mu'tazila argument through Al-Ka'bi. They assert that Allah sets a fixed lifespan, but then claim that a servant has power to prevent its fulfillment, thereby thwarting Allah's will and promise. This introduces a fundamental conflict between divine sovereignty and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation silently. Notice the direct accusation Al-Maturidi makes against the Mu'tazila.
The passage is a report of the Mu'tazila view, not an endorsement.
2
Identify the three parts of the claim
Find where Al-Maturidi states: (1) Allah sets a term, (2) Allah preserves life until that term, (3) But then humans have power to cut it short. Notice the contradiction.
3
Understand the logical consequences
The passage lists outcomes: breach of promise, coercion of God, etc. Ask yourself: why are these inevitable according to Al-Maturidi?
This prepares for the Muslim response in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الأجل. ثم سأل عن خصمه سؤالاً بَتَرَه، وإنما هو - والله أعلم - أن المعتزلة تزعم أن الله تعالى يجعل لعمر الرجل مدة بها ينقضى، وإبقاؤه إلى تلك المدّة فعله، وهو يريد أن يفعل ذلك، وقد كان قدر له في تلك المدة أرزاقا. ثم أثبتت لعبد من عبيد الله قوة يمنع بها ذلك الرجل عن استيفاء مدته التي جعلها الله له، ويمنع رب العالمين عن إنجاز ما وعده، ويحول بينه وبين فعله من إبقاء حياته في جسده وهو يريد ذلك؛ فيكون من فعله بما يقتل به عدوه منعا منه ربه، فيكون في ذلك خلف الوعد، وقهر ومنع عن فعله، وكل ذلك يكون بما أقدره هو عليه. وذلك لعجز وخلف سنة في المعقول أو لا؟ أجاب كجواب المسلمين أن المسألة فيما يقال في كل أمر: لو لم يكن ذلك كيف كان في علم الله أن يكون؟ وذلك القول عند المسلمين على تحقيق أن الكائن في علمه ذلك، فإن كان في وقدرته أن له أن يجعل في الابتداء غير تلك المدة، ولو جعل ليكون ذلك في علمه، لا هذا الذي كان. ثم رجع إلى حقيقة قوله وقال: لو كان الظالم إنما قتل الحضور أجله لم يكن ملوما، وقد يحمد أيضاً في ذبح شاة لآخر، إذ لولا ذبحه لكانت تموت. ثم عورض بأنك تشهد أنه لم يحضره أجله لو لم يقتله. فقال: معاذ الله، بل لعله يقتله غيره أو ينقضى أجله. ثم احتج بقوله عز وجل: {ولا يعمر من معمر ولا ينقص من عمره إلا في كتاب} [فاطر، ١١/٣٥] الآية، وبقول رسول الله عليه السلام: «صلة الرحم تزيد في العمر»، فأخبر أن له مقدارا معلوما يزيد فيه بالصلة، فيكون في اللوح: إن وصل فأجله كذا وإن لم يصل فكذا. ثم رجع إلى سفهه وعارض بالموهوم المطلق فإن قيل: في ذلك دفع المنع لا غير، وفي القدرة الفعل؟ فقال: وفي القدرة دفع العجز لا غير. وقال: لو أوجبت القدرة لأدخلت فيه ولحملت عليه، ويكون إذ ذاك الفعل لغيري. أي إن الكعبي قد وجه لنفسه سؤالاً على لسان خصمه حيث أسكت به نفسه.
The issue of the term. Then he asked his opponent a question that cut him off, and it is – and God knows best – that the Mu'tazila claim that God Almighty sets a period for a man's life by which it ends, and keeping him to that period is His act, and He wants to do that, and He has decreed provisions for him in that period. Then they posited for a servant of God's servants a power by which he prevents that man from fulfilling his term which God has set for him, and prevents the Lord of the worlds from fulfilling what He promised, and comes between Him and His act of preserving his life in his body while He wants that; so it would be from his act of killing his enemy a prevention from his Lord, and in that would be a breach of promise, and coercion and prevention from His act, and all that would be by what He empowered him to do. That is due to incapacity and a violation of the norm in reason, or not? He answered with the answer of the Muslims: the issue is like what is said in every matter: if it were not so, how would it be in God's knowledge that it be? And that statement according to the Muslims is based on the verification that what exists in His knowledge is that, and if it is in His power that He can initially set a different period, and if He had set it, it would be in His knowledge, not this one that occurred. Then he returned to the reality of his statement and said: if the oppressor only killed [someone] at the arrival of his term, he would not be blameworthy, and he might even be praised in slaughtering a sheep for another, since if he had not slaughtered it, it would have died. Then he was objected: you witness that he would not have reached his term if he had not killed him. He said: God forbid, but perhaps someone else would kill him or his term would expire. Then he argued with the saying of Allah Almighty: {No person grows old nor is his life decreased except in a book} [Fatir, 35:11], the verse, and with the saying of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him: 'Maintenance of family ties increases lifespan,' so he informed that he has a known amount which increases by maintaining ties, so it is in the tablet: if he maintains ties, his term is such; if he does not, it is such. Then he returned to his foolishness and objected with the absolute imaginary; if it is said: in that is a repulsion of prevention only, and in ability is the act? He said: and in ability is a repulsion of inability only. And he said: if ability necessitated [action], I would bring it into it and be compelled to it, and then the act would be from other than me. That is, Al-Ka'bi posed a question to himself on the tongue of his opponent, thereby silencing himself.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument (through Al-Ka'bi) that a person can prevent the lifespan that Allah has set, and the Muslim response. It begins by stating the Mu'tazila claim: Allah sets a fixed lifespan for a person, but then they posit that a servant has power to prevent that lifespan from being fulfilled, thereby thwarting Allah's will and promise. This leads to contradictions: breach of promise, coercion of God, etc. The Muslim response is to frame the issue in terms of divine knowledge: the question is about what is in God's knowledge. The Muslim view is that whatever occurs is in God's knowledge; if God's power allows a different initial setting, that would have been what is in His knowledge. Al-Ka'bi then retreats by arguing that if someone kills another at the end of his life, it would not be blameworthy, even commendable like slaughtering a sheep that would die anyway. He is countered: you witness that he would not have died then if not killed. Al-Ka'bi replies that perhaps another would kill him or his term would expire anyway. He then cites the Quranic verse from Surah Fatir (35:11) and the hadith 'Silat ar-rahim increases lifespan' as evidence that the term is changeable. He interprets that the tablet contains conditional statements: if he maintains ties, term X; if not, term Y. The passage concludes by noting that Al-Ka'bi fell into absurdity by arguing that power is only the ability to prevent inability, and that if ability necessitated action, he would be compelled, making the act from another. The editor notes that Al-Ka'bi silenced himself with his own argument.

Key Terms
أجل
العمر
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans have independent power to override Allah's decree without contradiction.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows this leads to absurdities like breaching Allah's promise and coercing Him.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila claim, what does the servant's power enable him to do?
A) Fulfill his own lifespan
B) Prevent the divinely appointed lifespan from being completed
C) Alter the provisions Allah decreed
D) Create a new lifespan

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim that humans can block Allah's decreed lifespan leads to logical absurdities, especially regarding divine power and promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans for your life, do you truly believe that Allah's decree is unchallengeable, or is there a part of you that assumes your efforts can overcome His will? Confront this passage: what would it mean for you to live as if your will could override Allah's?
?
This passage exposes the absurdity of claiming power against Allah. In what areas of your life have you subtly asserted autonomy from Allah's will—perhaps in your career, relationships, or health? What specific step will you take this week to consciously submit your plans to His decree?
?
If you truly internalized that no human power can prevent Allah from fulfilling His promise, how would it change your response to your own failures or the actions of others who wrong you? Are you holding onto resentment as if they could thwart Allah's plan?

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
The Muslim Response: Divine Knowledge as the Framework
Al-Maturidi provides the Muslim refutation of Al-Ka'bi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muslim response to the Mu'tazila argument: that the issue is framed by divine knowledge and power, not human interference.

Core Idea
The Muslim response: whatever occurs is in Allah's knowledge and power from eternity.

After presenting the Mu'tazila argument, Al-Maturidi gives the Muslim answer. Instead of engaging with human power, the response reframes the entire issue by referring to divine knowledge: the question is about what is in Allah's knowledge and what He can initially set. This shifts the discussion from human agency to divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Muslim answer
Read the passage beginning with 'He answered with the answer of the Muslims.' Notice how it avoids direct confrontation with the Mu'tazila premise.
The answer uses a hypothetical: 'if it were not so, how would it be in God's knowledge?'
2
Understand the role of divine knowledge
The passage says: what exists in Allah's knowledge is what occurs. If Allah's power could set a different term, that would be in His knowledge instead.
This implies that the actual term is what Allah knew and willed.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila approach
Compare this response to the Mu'tazila emphasis on human ability. The Muslim answer neutralizes the human power argument by returning to Allah's eternal knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Muslim response merely denies human power without a positive framework.
Correction: The response provides a positive framework: divine knowledge as the determinant of what occurs.

Checkpoint
What is the core principle of the Muslim response according to Al-Maturidi?
A) Humans have no power at all
B) Whatever occurs is what Allah knew would occur
C) The lifespan can change based on actions
D) Allah is not responsible for human actions

Key Takeaway
The Muslim response shifts the debate from human power to divine knowledge, affirming that what actually happens is precisely what Allah knew from eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your choices are irrelevant because Allah already knows everything? How does the Muslim response in this lesson challenge that feeling?
?

?
This lesson shows that Allah's knowledge includes all possible alternatives. Does knowing that He could have decreed a different life for you make you more grateful for what He actually decreed? What should change in how you make du'a and take action?

Refuting Al-Ka'bi: Blame, Scripture, and Contradiction
Al-Maturidi refutes Al-Ka'bi's remaining arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Maturidi refutes Al-Ka'bi's arguments concerning blame, the Quranic verse on the book of decrees, and the hadith on maintaining family ties.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi's own arguments about blame and conditional decrees lead to inconsistency and absurdity.

Al-Maturidi continues his refutation by examining Al-Ka'bi's attempts to defend his position. Al-Ka'bi argues that if killing occurs at the end of the term, the killer is not blameworthy, and he uses a hadith to claim that lifespan can increase through good deeds. Al-Maturidi shows that these arguments backfire, revealing that Al-Ka'bi ultimately defeats himself.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Ka'bi's blame argument
Al-Ka'bi says that if a killer kills at the exact end of a lifespan, he is not blameworthy, just as someone who slaughters a sheep that would die anyway is praised. Read this part carefully.
This is a defense of predestination that Al-Maturidi critiques.
2
Understand the objection and reply
The opponent objects: 'You witness that he would not have died then if not killed.' Al-Ka'bi replies that another might have killed him or his term would expire anyway.
This shows Al-Ka'bi's reliance on hypotheticals.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
Al-Ka'bi cites the Quran (35:11) and the hadith on silat ar-rahim. Notice how he interprets 'increase in lifespan' as a conditional decree in the Preserved Tablet.
Al-Maturidi uses this to show that Al-Ka'bi's own evidence contradicts his earlier claim of a fixed term.
4
See the final contradiction
The passage ends by noting that Al-Ka'bi's argument about ability leads to absurdity: if ability necessitated action, then the act would be from another. Al-Maturidi concludes that Al-Ka'bi silenced himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الأجل. ثم سأل عن خصمه سؤالاً بَتَرَه، وإنما هو - والله أعلم - أن المعتزلة تزعم أن الله تعالى يجعل لعمر الرجل مدة بها ينقضى، وإبقاؤه إلى تلك المدّة فعله، وهو يريد أن يفعل ذلك، وقد كان قدر له في تلك المدة أرزاقا. ثم أثبتت لعبد من عبيد الله قوة يمنع بها ذلك الرجل عن استيفاء مدته التي جعلها الله له، ويمنع رب العالمين عن إنجاز ما وعده، ويحول بينه وبين فعله من إبقاء حياته في جسده وهو يريد ذلك؛ فيكون من فعله بما يقتل به عدوه منعا منه ربه، فيكون في ذلك خلف الوعد، وقهر ومنع عن فعله، وكل ذلك يكون بما أقدره هو عليه. وذلك لعجز وخلف سنة في المعقول أو لا؟ أجاب كجواب المسلمين أن المسألة فيما يقال في كل أمر: لو لم يكن ذلك كيف كان في علم الله أن يكون؟ وذلك القول عند المسلمين على تحقيق أن الكائن في علمه ذلك، فإن كان في وقدرته أن له أن يجعل في الابتداء غير تلك المدة، ولو جعل ليكون ذلك في علمه، لا هذا الذي كان. ثم رجع إلى حقيقة قوله وقال: لو كان الظالم إنما قتل الحضور أجله لم يكن ملوما، وقد يحمد أيضاً في ذبح شاة لآخر، إذ لولا ذبحه لكانت تموت. ثم عورض بأنك تشهد أنه لم يحضره أجله لو لم يقتله. فقال: معاذ الله، بل لعله يقتله غيره أو ينقضى أجله. ثم احتج بقوله عز وجل: {ولا يعمر من معمر ولا ينقص من عمره إلا في كتاب} [فاطر، ١١/٣٥] الآية، وبقول رسول الله عليه السلام: «صلة الرحم تزيد في العمر»، فأخبر أن له مقدارا معلوما يزيد فيه بالصلة، فيكون في اللوح: إن وصل فأجله كذا وإن لم يصل فكذا. ثم رجع إلى سفهه وعارض بالموهوم المطلق فإن قيل: في ذلك دفع المنع لا غير، وفي القدرة الفعل؟ فقال: وفي القدرة دفع العجز لا غير. وقال: لو أوجبت القدرة لأدخلت فيه ولحملت عليه، ويكون إذ ذاك الفعل لغيري. أي إن الكعبي قد وجه لنفسه سؤالاً على لسان خصمه حيث أسكت به نفسه.

The editor notes that Al-Ka'bi silenced himself with his own argument.

Misconception: That Al-Ka'bi's arguments are consistent; in fact, he contradicts himself and is refuted by his own logic.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that Al-Ka'bi's own reasoning leads to absurdity and self-silence.

What conclusion does Al-Maturidi draw about Al-Ka'bi's argument at the end?
A) Al-Ka'bi has successfully defended his position
B) Al-Ka'bi silenced himself with his own question
C) Al-Ka'bi's argument is based on sound reasoning
D) Al-Ka'bi accepts the Muslim response

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi shows that Al-Ka'bi's arguments about blame and conditional decrees are self-defeating, ultimately leading to his own silence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you sometimes justify it to yourself by saying, 'It was meant to be'? In light of this lesson, why is that excuse invalid and what does it reveal about your view of your own responsibility? Commit to calling your actions your own, without blaming qadar.
?
Think of a situation where you blamed someone else for an outcome that you also contributed to. Al-Maturidi shows that the killer is blameworthy even if the victim's term had arrived. Are you willing to accept full responsibility for your part, even when the outcome seems 'destined'? How can you practice taking ownership today?
?
This lesson shows that Al-Ka'bi's arguments led him to silence himself. Is there an area of your life where you have been using intellectual arguments to avoid a deeper truth about your own accountability? Will you stop hiding behind theology and face what you already know?

Conditional Decrees and Divine Knowledge
Maturidi critiques conditional decrees and distinguishes two tablets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects conditional decrees in the Preserved Tablet and how they reflect human ignorance, not divine uncertainty.

Core Idea
Conditional decrees reveal human ignorance, not divine uncertainty.

Maturidi begins by exposing the flaw in Al-Ka'bi's argument about conditional decrees. He argues that if God knows the outcome, He does not write 'if this, then that' but rather the actual result. The conditional style belongs to those ignorant of consequences. This lesson unpacks that critique and introduces the key distinction between the Preserved Tablet and the 'tablet of every ship.'

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's opening remarks
Read the first few sentences where Maturidi states that Al-Ka'bi's deviation is clear and that the extent of his knowledge is limited.
Notice the tone: Maturidi is sharply critical but also sympathetic ('so that they may know his excuse').
2
Follow the key question about God's knowledge
Identify the question: Did God know the person would be killed? Trace the logical consequences that Maturidi draws from the answer.
3
Understand the critique of conditional writing
Maturidi argues that writing conditional outcomes in the tablet is the act of one who does not know the future. See how he contrasts that with the one who knows—who writes the actual outcome.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Distinguish the two tablets
Note the terms: 'Preserved Tablet' vs. 'tablet of every ship' (a metaphor for human speculative reasoning). Maturidi calls the latter 'the lost tablet.'
This distinction is crucial for later kalam discussions.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how Maturidi's argument upholds the perfection of divine knowledge: God knows all outcomes with certainty, so conditional decrees are meaningless for Him.
This connects to the broader theme of 'ilm Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: من تأمل ما قال وما قوبل به أيقن أنه حائد حد عن الجواب. لكنا نذكر غفلته فيما حايد به ليعلموا عذره في جميع ما فارق به خصومه؛ إذ هذا مبلغ علمه في الله سبحانه. ونقول له: الله علم أنه يُقتل أو لا؟ فإن قال: يعلم، قيل: وقتله يزيل حياته ويذهب عمره أو لا؟ فإن قال لا كذبه الوجود وإن قال: نعم، قيل: كيف جعل انقضاء عمره وخروج روحه من جسده بغيره، لو علم ذلك؟ وكيف كتب في اللوح أنه إن فعل كذا يكون كذا، وإن لم يفعل كذا يكون كذا؟ وهذا أمر من لا يعلم ما يكون. فأما من يعلم ما يكون فهو يكتب: يكون كذا، ولولا أنه يكون كان كذا؛ وكذا يكفر فلان مقت الله ولولا أنه يكفر كان يؤمن ويستوجب محبة الله. فأما القول بـ يكون ذا أو ذا من غير القطع بما يكون إنما هو فعل الجهال بالعواقب. ثم أنّى يكون ذا خبرًا عن علم يثبته قبل كونه؟ وكل الناس يعلمون هذا القدر: إن فلاناً إما يُقتل أو يموت، يؤمن أو يكفر، يتحرك في وقت كذا أو يسكن. فهذا القدر من اللوح هو لوح كل سفينة وليس هو اللوح المحفوظ، ولكنه اللوح المضيع. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله: لو حضر أجله، فإن أجله ليس يُغيَّر بالقتل فيما كان في علم الله، وهو كما في علمه أنه يقتل، ولكنه بالقتل المنهى عنه أو المأمور به على ما في علم الله، وهو كما في علمه أنه يؤمن ويكفر، فذلك في علمه وكل داخل فيما علم الله عاقبته أنه إلى ماذا يرجع، وإن كان في علمه أنه لو لم يفعل ذلك ماذا تكون عاقبته أنه إلى ماذا يرجع، فمثله الأجل. وعلى ذلك إذ علم الله أنه يصل رحمه فجعل عمره أكثر مما كان في علمه أنه لا يصل، وكذلك أمر الآية؛ إذ محال أن يكون ما يفعله خارجا من علمه، والذي قالوا هو ذلك في المعقول. وللآية قال أهل التأويل: تبين منتهى عمره وتدل على نقصان كل وقت يمضى من عمره. وقال قوم: إنما هو في مختلف أعمار الخلق من بين مطوّل ومقصر، لا أن الله يجعل لأحد عمرا ثم
Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Whoever contemplates what he said and what was responded to him will be certain that he deviated away from the answer. But we will mention his negligence in what he deviated with so that they may know his excuse in all that he differed from his opponents; for this is the extent of his knowledge regarding God, the Exalted. And we say to him: Did God know that he would be killed or not? If he says: He knows, it is said: And does his killing remove his life and take away his lifespan or not? If he says no, reality belies him; if he says yes, it is said: How did He make the expiry of his life and the departure of his soul from his body by another, if He knew that? And how did He write in the tablet that if he does such, it will be such, and if he does not do such, it will be such? This is the affair of one who does not know what will be. As for the one who knows what will be, he writes: it will be such, and were it not that it will be, it would be such; and likewise, such-and-such disbelieves incurring the wrath of God, and were it not that he disbelieves, he would believe and deserve the love of God. As for the statement that it will be this or that without certainty of what will be, it is only the action of the ignorant of consequences. Then how can this be a report about knowledge that He established before its occurrence? And all people know this much: that such-and-such is either killed or dies, believes or disbelieves, moves at such a time or stays still. This amount of the tablet is the tablet of every ship, and it is not the Preserved Tablet, but it is the lost tablet. And there is no power except with God. And his saying: if his term had arrived, then his term is not changed by killing in what was in God's knowledge, and it is as in His knowledge that he would be killed, but it is by the prohibited killing or the commanded killing according to what is in God's knowledge, and it is as in His knowledge that he believes or disbelieves, so that is in His knowledge, and everything enters into what God knows its consequence, that it returns to what, and if it is in His knowledge that if he had not done that, what would be its consequence that it returns to what, so likewise is the term. And in that, when God knew that he would maintain his family ties, He made his lifespan longer than what was in His knowledge that he would not maintain ties. And likewise is the matter of the verse, since it is impossible that what He does is outside His knowledge, and what they said is that in reason. And regarding the verse, the people of interpretation said: it clarifies the end of his life and indicates the decrease of every moment that passes from his life. And some people said: it is about the different lifespans of creatures, between long-lived and short-lived, not that God appoints a lifespan for someone and then
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's direct refutation of Al-Ka'bi's earlier argument. He begins by noting that anyone who reflects on Al-Ka'bi's statements and the responses will see that he avoided the true answer. Maturidi then points out Al-Ka'bi's negligence so that readers may understand his excuse—that this was the extent of his knowledge about God. Maturidi poses a key question: Did God know that the person would be killed or not? If Al-Ka'bi says God knew, then does the killing end his life? If he says no, reality contradicts; if yes, then how could God have made his life end by another's action if He knew that? This shows the decree is fixed. Maturidi then critiques the idea of conditional writing in the tablet ('if he does X, then Y') as the act of one who does not know the outcome. One who knows the outcome writes the actual result. Conditional statements are for the ignorant. Maturidi calls such conditional knowledge 'the tablet of every ship' (a metaphor for speculative human reasoning), not the Preserved Tablet. He then explains that the term (ajal) is not changed by killing, as it is according to God's knowledge. The hadith about silat ar-rahim increasing lifespan is understood as God knowing two possibilities: if he maintains ties, his lifespan is longer; if not, it is shorter—both are known to God. The verse from Fatir is interpreted by scholars as clarifying the end of one's life and the decrease of time, or as referring to the different lifespans of people, not a change in a fixed decree. Maturidi concludes that it is impossible for anything to occur outside God's knowledge.

Key Terms
علم الله
اللوح المحفوظ
اللوح المضيع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that conditional decrees in the tablet indicate that God's knowledge changes or is uncertain.
Correction: Maturidi explains that conditional language reflects human ignorance; God's knowledge is absolute and writes the definite outcome.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the 'tablet of every ship' represent?
A) The Preserved Tablet (al-Lawh al-Mahfuz)
B) The lost tablet (al-Lawh al-Mudi')
C) A metaphor for the knowledge of the ignorant about consequences
D) The tablet that contains all divine decrees

Key Takeaway
Conditional decrees are incompatible with God's perfect knowledge; they only reflect human ignorance and are not part of the Preserved Tablet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your life with 'if this, then that' contingencies, do you realize that such thinking betrays an underlying assumption that God's decree is uncertain? How can you replace conditional planning with trust in the certainty of divine knowledge?
?
Have you ever, even subconsciously, thought that your actions could change God's decision as if He were waiting to see what you would do? How does Maturidi's argument about the Preserved Tablet confront this subtle form of shirk?
?
Do you live as though your destiny is already written with absolute certainty, or do you find yourself living in the 'lost tablet' of human speculation? What specific habit can you change today to submit more fully to God's definitive decree?

The Fixed Term and Silat al-Rahim
Maturidi explains the fixity of the term and the meaning of silat al-rahim and the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's explanation that the term (ajal) is fixed in God's knowledge and that silat al-rahim (maintaining family ties) does not change the decree but is part of it.

Core Idea
God's knowledge fixes the term; no action, even silat al-rahim, changes it.

In the continuation, Maturidi addresses the objection that killing seems to shorten life and the hadith that silat al-rahim increases lifespan. He argues that the term is not changed by killing because it is exactly as God knows it. Similarly, silat al-rahim is known by God as the cause for a longer lifespan, but that longer lifespan was already decreed. He also offers interpretations of the Qur'anic verse (35:11) about lifespans, clarifying that it does not imply change in God's decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the term (ajal)
Read from 'Wa qawluhu: law hadara ajaluhu' through the discussion of the verse. Notice how Maturidi defends the fixity of the term.
The passage is incomplete; Maturidi's thought is cut off mid-sentence.
2
Analyze the example of killing
Maturidi says the term is not changed by killing; it is as God knows. The act of killing itself, whether prohibited or commanded, is within God's knowledge.
This reinforces the idea that God's knowledge is comprehensive and unchanging.
3
Understand silat al-rahim and lifespan
Maturidi explains that when God knows a person will maintain family ties, He decrees a longer lifespan. This does not mean the actual decree changes; both possibilities are known to God.
This addresses a common difficulty with the hadith.
4
Consider the interpretation of the verse
Maturidi mentions two interpretations: some say the verse clarifies the end of life and the decrease of time; others say it refers to different lifespans decreed for different people.
Both interpretations avoid the notion of changing a fixed decree.
5
Reflect on the overarching theme
How does Maturidi's explanation preserve both divine knowledge and human responsibility?
This lesson connects to broader issues of qadar and divine attributes.

?

The Fixity of Lifespan: Maturidi's Refutation of Increase or Decrease
Maturidi refutes the claim that lifespan can be increased or decreased.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions like maintaining family ties cannot alter the fixed lifespan decreed by Allah.

Core Idea
Human actions cannot alter the fixed lifespan decreed by Allah.

Maturidi tackles a common belief that maintaining family ties (silat al-rahim) can increase one's lifespan. He argues that this contradicts the Quranic decree that when one's term comes, it cannot be delayed or advanced. Through a dialectical argument, he shows that any claim of increase or decrease leads to absurdities about God's knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice Maturidi's tone and the structure of his argument.
The passage begins with characterizing the opposing view as ignorance.
2
Identify the Quranic proof text
Maturidi cites verse 10:49 of Surah Yunus. Reflect on how this verse supports the fixity of the appointed time of death.
The verse explicitly states that when the term comes, it cannot be delayed or advanced.
3
Trace the dialectical argument
Follow the three options Maturidi presents to the opponent and the consequences of each. Pay attention to the logical progression.
The argument forces the opponent into a dilemma: either they accuse God of ignorance or of falsehood.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this argument safeguards God's attributes of knowledge and truthfulness.
Maturidi shows that the only coherent position is that lifespan is fixed and known to God from eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبدو له فيزيد أو ينقص كفعل الجهال ومن في أمورهم على شك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والله تعالى يقول : "إذا جاء أجلهم فلا يستأخرون ساعة ولا يستقدمون" [يونس، ٤٩/١٠]، وفيما يقول لا يجئ أجلهم بل يُقتلون قبل مجيء أجلهم. والله أيضاً لا يزيد في العمر، وكيف يقدر أن يزيد في عمر آخر بصلة الرَّحِم من لم يقدر بإيفاء ما ضمن من عمره أن يبقيه إلى وقت وكذا، بل قدر عدوه حتى منعه عن ذلك. جلّ الله عن هذا الوصف. ثم يقال له : ما ضَرَب من المدة له ألا كان في اللوح أنه يُبقيه إلى ذلك، أو يبقى هو إلى ذلك، أو يُبقيه ويبقى إن لم يُقتل؟ فإن قال بالأول والثاني ادعى عليه الكذب في خبره والخلف في وعده. وإن قال بالثالث قيل : أكان يعلم أنه يُقتل أو لا ؟ فإن قال : لا ، استحق الإبانة بين رأسه وجسده والخلود في عذاب ربه. وإن قال: نعم، قيل : لِمَ كتب ما لا يعلم؟ إذ ذلك في العرف صنيع الجهال مما تأبى عقول من عرف الرب التفوه به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
It appears to him that he increases or decreases, like the action of the ignorant and those who are in doubt about their affairs. There is no power except with Allah. Allah the Exalted says: 'When their term comes, they cannot delay it for an hour nor advance it.' [Yunus 10:49], and in what He says: their term does not come, rather they are killed before the coming of their term. And Allah also does not increase in lifespan. How can He increase in another's lifespan through silat al-rahim, while He is not able to fulfill what He has guaranteed of his lifespan to keep him alive until a time? But rather, He empowered his enemy to prevent him from that. Exalted is Allah above this description. Then it is said to him: What duration did He set for him that it was not in the Preserved Tablet that He would keep him until that, or that he would remain until that, or that He would keep him and he would remain if he is not killed? If he says the first or the second, he has accused Him of falsehood in His news and breaking His promise. If he says the third, it is said: Did He know that he would be killed or not? If he says: No, he deserves the severance between his head and his body and eternal punishment in the torment of his Lord. If he says: Yes, it is said: Why did He write what He does not know? For that, according to custom, is the action of the ignorant, which the minds of those who know the Lord refuse to utter. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that human actions—such as silat al-rahim (maintaining family ties)—can increase one's lifespan or that one can be killed before their appointed time. Maturidi begins by characterizing such a view as the action of the ignorant and those in doubt. He then cites the Quranic verse (Yunus 10:49) that when a person's term arrives, it cannot be delayed or advanced, implying that the appointed time of death is fixed. He argues that it is impossible for God to increase a lifespan if He has already guaranteed a specific duration, because that would imply He is unable to fulfill His guarantee. Instead, the person is killed by an enemy, and God's power is in preventing or allowing that. Maturidi then presents a dialectical argument: If one says that the duration was not predetermined, that implies God's ignorance, which is blasphemy. If one says that God knew the person would be killed, then why did He write something contrary to His knowledge? That would be the act of the ignorant. Thus, the only coherent position is that God's decree is fixed and known, and human actions do not alter it.

Key Terms
أجل
صلة الرحم
اللوح
الكذب
خلف الوعد
علم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that silat al-rahim (maintaining family ties) literally changes the predestined lifespan.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's decree is immutable; human actions do not alter what is already written in the Preserved Tablet.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows if someone claims that God did not know whether a person would be killed?
A) He is merely mistaken but not a disbeliever.
B) He deserves the severance of his head and eternal punishment.
C) He must be corrected but not punished.
D) He is excused because of ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that lifespan is fixed in God's decree and human actions cannot alter it; any claim otherwise leads to blasphemy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own death, do you trust that its timing is completely in Allah's hands, or do you find yourself trying to control it through precautions and plans? What does that reveal about your reliance on His decree?
?
If you truly believed that no good deed—including silat al-rahim—can add a single day to your life, would you still perform them with the same urgency? Or does your heart harbor a hidden hope that they will 'buy' you more time?
?
When someone dies young or suddenly, do you internally question Allah's wisdom or feel that their term was 'unfair'? How can you train your heart to submit fully to the fixity of the decree, even when it seems tragic?

The Contradiction in the Mu'tazilite Position on Prevention and Compulsion
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument that God prevents actions He knows will not occur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes a logical contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position on divine prevention and compulsion.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite argument for prevention logically forces them into the very compulsion they reject.

In this passage, Maturidi engages in a dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God prevents actions He knows will not occur. He shows that their own reasoning, when consistently applied, leads to the conclusion that all good and evil are by God's prevention and compulsion — precisely the view they oppose. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for the debate on human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, trying to grasp the logical flow of the argument.
Notice that Maturidi starts with a concrete scenario involving someone God knows will not be killed.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premise
What do the Mu'tazilites claim about prevention? Maturidi takes their claim and applies it consistently.
The key is understanding that they say God prevents actions He knows will not occur.
3
Follow the reductio
See how Maturidi shows that the premise leads to its opposite: that all good and evil are by God's prevention and compulsion.
This conclusion is exactly what the Mu'tazilites reject, thus their position is self-contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يُعارَض بمن علم الله أنه لا يُقتل ويريدون قتله ويؤثرونه ويقصدون قصده بجميع ما يحتمله وسعهم، ثم يكون على ما علم وهذه أسباب لا تجد أحداً تكون منه لا يقع الفعل به، وفي الوقوع كذبه إلا أن يقول : يمنع، فيلزمه في كل ما يعلم الله أنه لا يكون المنع مع القوة . وإذا لزم ذلك لزم الدفع في كل ما يعلم أنه يكون إذا لم يرض به العبد. فيكون كل خير وشر بالمنع والدفع الذي ظنوا به أن قول خصومهم يؤدي إليه، وهو الذي حملهم على رأيهم . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then one counters with the one whom God knows will not be killed, yet they want to kill him, and they seek him out and intend his intention with all that they can, then it happens according to what He knows. These are causes that you do not find anyone from whom the action does not occur, and in its occurrence there is falsehood, unless he says: He prevents. Then it becomes necessary for him in everything that God knows will not happen that prevention is with power. And if that is necessary, then prevention is necessary in everything that He knows will happen if the servant does not consent to it. Thus, all good and evil are by prevention and compulsion, which they thought leads to the saying of their opponents, and it is that which drove them to their view. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a counter-argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that God prevents actions that He knows will not occur. Maturidi points out a contradiction: if God knows that a person will not be killed, yet people try to kill him and he is not killed, the Mu'tazilite would say that God prevented the killing. But if prevention is with power, then it must apply to everything God knows will not happen, including actions the servant does not consent to. This leads to the conclusion that all good and evil are by God's prevention and compulsion, which is exactly the position the Mu'tazilite opposes. Thus, their own reasoning forces them into the view they reject.

Key Terms
منع
دفع
قوة
علم الله
فعل العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite concept of divine prevention avoids implying compulsion.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazilite position, when consistently applied, entails that all good and evil are by God's prevention and compulsion.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim follows from the Mu'tazilite view that God prevents actions He knows will not happen?
A) That God's knowledge is limited.
B) That all good and evil are by God's prevention and compulsion.
C) That human free will is absolute.
D) That God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite doctrine of prevention logically leads to the very compulsion they deny, refuting their position from within.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a position to avoid what you thought was a dangerous belief, only to realize that your own view leads to the same conclusion? What does this reveal about your attachment to your own logic over God's revelation?
?
This passage shows that even sincere scholars can build a system on a premise that contradicts itself. How comfortable are you with the possibility that some of your cherished beliefs about God's decree might be inconsistent? What would it take for you to abandon them?
?
The Mu'tazilites were driven by a desire to preserve human responsibility, but their logic forced them into compulsion. Consider an area in your life where you cling to control despite Islamic teachings. How might your attempt to preserve a sense of freedom actually distance you from true tawakkul and submission to Allah's knowledge and power?

The Three Aspects of Absolute Permission
Maturidi enumerates the three aspects of absolute permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of the term 'iṭlāq wa takhliya' as explained by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Absolute permission has three meanings: removal of hindrance, command, or permissibility.

Before discussing how permission applies to good and evil, we must first clarify what 'absolute permission' means. Maturidi shows that the term is not univocal but has three possible interpretations, each with distinct implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that defines the three aspects of iṭlāq wa takhliya. Note the three phrases: rafʿ al-ʿusr wa al-manʿ, al-amr bihi, and al-ibāḥa.
These are the three ways to understand 'permission'.
2
Understand each aspect
For each aspect, think of an example: removal of hardship (e.g., permission to eat when fasting is harmful), command (e.g., commanding something as a divine directive), permissibility (e.g., declaring something allowed without command or prohibition).
3
Reflect on the scope
Notice that Maturidi presents these as the possible meanings of the term. This sets the stage for the next discussion about good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من الإطلاق والتخلية فهو كلام يتوجه أوجها ثلاثة: رفع العسر والمنع، أو الأمر به، أو الإباحة . وذلك كله في الخير مطلق وفي الشر لا إلا مقيداً : إنه لم يُقسر ولم يُجبَر. وإذا كان كذلك فمعارضته بالذي ذكر فاسد . وما أجاب عنا بالمنع فحق. قال الله تعالى في قوله: فخلوا سبيلهم بعد ذكر المنع ، وما يحمد من قول الناس: «اللهم قونا على طاعتك»، ولا يحمد : «اللهم خلّ بيننا وبين طاعتك»؛ ثبت أن لأحدهما حالاً ليس للاخر. وكذلك هو يقول بالفعل وقت فناء القدرة ولا قدرة معه، ولا يقول بارتفاع الإطلاق والتخلية وقت هذا الفعل لتعلم بذلك بعده فيها قدر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And what is mentioned of absolute permission (al-iṭlāq wa al-takhliya) is a statement that has three aspects: removal of hardship and prevention, or commanding it, or permissibility. And all of that is unconditional in good, but in evil it is only qualified: that he is not coerced and not compelled. And if that is so, then opposing it with what was mentioned is corrupt. And what he answered us with prevention is true. Allah the Exalted said in His words: 'so let them go free' after mentioning prevention, and what is praised from people's saying: 'O Allah, help us to obey You', and it is not praised: 'O Allah, let us free to obey You'; it is established that one has a state that the other does not. And likewise, he says about the action at the time of the depletion of power and there is no power with it, and he does not say the removal of absolute permission at the time of this action, so that you know by that afterward that there is power in it. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the term 'iṭlāq wa takhliya' (absolute permission). Maturidi explains that it has three meanings: (1) removal of hardship and prevention, (2) command, or (3) permissibility. In good actions, all three apply unconditionally. In evil actions, they only apply with the qualification that the agent is not coerced or compelled. Maturidi then refutes a possible objection: the Mu'tazilite argument based on 'so let them go free' (from Quran 9:5) is corrupt because the context includes a prior command to kill. He also contrasts two supplications: 'help us to obey' is praiseworthy, while 'let us free to obey' is not, because the latter implies lack of divine assistance. Finally, he notes that even when human power is depleted, the action still occurs by God's power, and this does not negate the reality of human acquisition (kasb).

Key Terms
إطلاق
تخلية
رفع العسر
المنع
الأمر
الإباحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many aspects does 'iṭlāq wa takhliya' have?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Absolute permission (iṭlāq wa takhliya) can mean removing hindrance, commanding, or permitting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you believed Allah had given you 'free rein' in a decision. Based on this lesson, in which of the three senses—removal of hardship, command, or permissibility—was that permission actually given? How does that change your responsibility before Him?
?
The term 'command' is listed as a meaning of absolute permission. Have you ever considered an obligation as a form of permission? How might that perspective change your willingness and gratitude in fulfilling Allah's commands?
?
Maturidi distinguishes between 'removal of hardship' and 'permissibility.' When has Allah removed a hardship from you that you previously thought was a prohibition? Does that shift your understanding of His mercy, and what action does that demand from you today?

Good and Evil: Unconditional vs Qualified Permission
Maturidi differentiates between unconditional permission in good and qualified permission in evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi says absolute permission is unconditional in good actions but qualified in evil actions.

Core Idea
Permission is unconditional for good but only qualified for evil, meaning no coercion.

Now that we know the three meanings of absolute permission, Maturidi applies this to the moral categories of good and evil. He asserts that in good actions, all three aspects apply without restriction, but in evil actions, they apply only with the qualification that the agent is not coerced.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule
Read the Arabic passage that states the rule: 'all of that is unconditional in good, but in evil it is only qualified.'
2
Understand the qualification for evil
The qualification is 'that he is not coerced and not compelled.' This means evil actions can be permitted only in the sense that God does not force them, not in any other sense.
3
Ask why this distinction matters
Consider why Maturidi makes this distinction. It preserves human responsibility while affirming God's control.
This connects to debates with the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من الإطلاق والتخلية فهو كلام يتوجه أوجها ثلاثة: رفع العسر والمنع، أو الأمر به، أو الإباحة . وذلك كله في الخير مطلق وفي الشر لا إلا مقيداً : إنه لم يُقسر ولم يُجبَر. وإذا كان كذلك فمعارضته بالذي ذكر فاسد . وما أجاب عنا بالمنع فحق. قال الله تعالى في قوله: فخلوا سبيلهم بعد ذكر المنع ، وما يحمد من قول الناس: «اللهم قونا على طاعتك»، ولا يحمد : «اللهم خلّ بيننا وبين طاعتك»؛ ثبت أن لأحدهما حالاً ليس للاخر. وكذلك هو يقول بالفعل وقت فناء القدرة ولا قدرة معه، ولا يقول بارتفاع الإطلاق والتخلية وقت هذا الفعل لتعلم بذلك بعده فيها قدر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And what is mentioned of absolute permission (al-iṭlāq wa al-takhliya) is a statement that has three aspects: removal of hardship and prevention, or commanding it, or permissibility. And all of that is unconditional in good, but in evil it is only qualified: that he is not coerced and not compelled. And if that is so, then opposing it with what was mentioned is corrupt. And what he answered us with prevention is true. Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the term 'iṭlāq wa takhliya' (absolute permission). Maturidi explains that it has three meanings: (1) removal of hardship and prevention, (2) command, or (3) permissibility. In good actions, all three apply unconditionally. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own sins, do you ever subtly excuse them by feeling that God 'allowed' them in the same way He allows good deeds? How does this lesson's distinction between unconditional and qualified permission force you to accept full responsibility for your evil choices, and what will you change today because of that?
?
If God's permission for evil is only to the extent that you are not forced, then every sin you commit is entirely your own doing. How does this truth sit with you? Will you let it drive you to despair, or to a deeper repentance and resolve to avoid evil starting now?
?
Think of a specific situation where you witnessed or experienced evil and struggled to understand God's justice. How does knowing that His permission was only qualified—that He did not approve or force it—reshape your trust in Him? What will you do the next time such a situation arises to affirm His justice rather than doubt it?

Refuting Objections and Clarifying the Role of Power
Maturidi refutes objections using Quran and supplication, and discusses power and permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazilite objection and his clarification that permission continues even when human power is depleted.

Core Idea
Opposing Maturidi's view is corrupt, and permission remains even when power vanishes.

After establishing the rule for good and evil, Maturidi addresses a likely objection: the Mu'tazilite use of the phrase 'so let them go free' from the Quran. He refutes it by noting the prior context of prevention. Then, he contrasts two supplications to show that divine assistance is needed, and finally, he explains that permission is not removed when human power is exhausted.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the lines starting from 'If that is so...' to the end. Identify the objection and Maturidi's response.
2
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The verse 'so let them go free' is quoted with the note 'after mentioning prevention.' Maturidi points out that the permission is conditional on prior prohibition.
3
Examine the supplication contrast
Contrast 'help us to obey' (praised) and 'let us free to obey' (not praised). The latter implies lack of need for God's help, which is wrong.
4
Understand the power discussion
At the end, Maturidi notes that even when human power is gone, the action occurs by God's power, and permission is not removed.
